A Formal Specication Language
for Generic Railway Control
Systems
Jacob B Hansen
Kongens Lyngby 2015
==PAGE:2==
Technical University of Denmark
Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science
Matematiktorvet, building 303B,
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark

==PAGE:3==
Summary (English)
The goal of the thesis is to dene a formal specication language that can
be used for specifying generic models for interlocking systems.
Furthermore
support for translating specications of this language into internal language of
a model checking tool should be supported. The use of the language should be
illustrated by an example.
For the project a language has been dened for specifying generic models for
state based systems such that it can be used for interlocking systems.
This
language is based on the RSL-SAL language.
This report gives an analysis of what considerations we have in connection with
the specication of a model for the future danish interlocking system.
This
analysis includes aspects of which constructs, the language should support, and
how these constructs can be handled in RSL-SAL. In connection with this, it is
described how RSL-SAL could be extended in order to support such constructs.
In particularly the RSL-SAL language has been extended to support arrays, and
the possibility of prioritizing transitions.
The report describes how the concrete syntax and inference rules for type check-
ing are extended to include the language extensions. Furthermore, the meaning
of these extensions are described by corresponding SAL expression.
How these changes are implemented in the RSL-SAL syntax and type checker
and in the RSL-SAL to SAL translator is also described in this report. Likewise
bug xes of the existing RSL-SAL tool is described.
==PAGE:4==
ii
The language for specifying systems is illustrated by a generic model for future
danish interlocking systems. 5 dierent examples are used for instantiating the
model and translating the instantiated models into SAL. SAL has been used
to check the instantiated models for these examples, where performance has
been measured. These results are compared to results of using the RT-Tester
model checker tool. The results indicate that the RT-Tester tool gives better
performance.
Lastly limitations and future work is described.
==PAGE:5==
Summary (Danish)
Målet for denne afhandling er at denere et sprog, der kan bruges til specicering
af generiske modeller for jernbane systemer. Endvidere skal der være support for
oversættelse af dette sprog over i det interne sprog af et model checker værktøj.
Desuden skal brugen af værktøjet illustreres ved et eksempel.
I dette projekt, er der blevet deneret et sprog for specicering af state baserede
systemer, sådan at sproget kan bruges til at denere generiske modeller for
jernbane systemer. Dette sprog er baseret på RSL-SAL.
Denne rapport giver en analyse af hvilke overvejelser, der er i forbindelse med
specicering af en generisk model for de fremtidige danske jernbanesystemer.
Denne analyse inkluderer aspekter af hvilke konstruktioner, det færdige sprog
skal understøtte, og hvordan disse konstruktioner kan håndteres i RSL-SAL. I
forbindelse med dette er det diskuteret, hvordan RSL-SAL kan udvides for at
understøtte sådanne konstruktioner. Specikt så er RSL-SAL blevet udvidet til
at håndtere arrays og muligheden for at prioritere transitioner.
Rapporten beskriver, hvordan den konkrete syntaks og inferens regler for type
check, er udvidet for at understøtte disse udvidelser. Endvidere er betydningen
af disse udvidelser beskrevet ved korresponderende udtryk i SAL.
Hvordan disse ændringer er implementeret i RSL-SAL syntaksen og type checker
samt i RSL-SAL til SAL oversætteren, er også beskrevet i rapporten. Ligeledes
giver rapporten en oversigt over x af bugs, der er blevet udført for det originale
RSL-SAL værktøj.
==PAGE:6==
iv
Det udviklede sprog for specicering af modeller, er illustreret af en generisk
model beskrivende fremtidige danske jernbane systemer. 5 forskellige eksempler
er blevet brugt til instantiere modellen i SAL. SAL er så blevet brugt til at
teste de instantierede modeller for disse 5 eksempler, hvor performance er blevet
målt. Disse resultater er blevet sammenlignet med resultater, hvor RT-Tester
model checker værktøj er blevet brugt. Disse resultater indikere, at RT-Tester
værktøjet performer bedre.
Slutteligt er begrænsninger samt eventuelt fremtidigt arbejde beskrevet.
==PAGE:7==
Preface
This thesis was prepared at DTU Compute at the Technical University of Den-
mark in fullment of the requirements for acquiring an M.Sc.
in Computer
Science and Engineering.
The thesis deals with the specication of a formal specication language that
can be used to specify generic models for future danish interlocking systems.
The thesis has been written in the period of 15th September 2014 to 15th
February 2015 under the supervision of associate professor Anne Elisabeth Hax-
thausen and is worth 30 ECTS credits.
The thesis consists of the following report with an associated CD that contains
specication les for the generic model and source and executable of the tool.
Lyngby, 15-February-2015
Jacob B Hansen

Acknowledgements
I would like to thank my supervisor Anne Elisabeth Haxthausen for guidance
and counseling throughout the project.
Furthermore I would like to thank Linh Vu Hong, ph.d. student at DTU, that
has worked with specifying the generic model for future danish interlocking
systems. Furthermore he has assisted in explaining the model and assisted in
running experiments. Linh has provided experimental results of checking the
model using the RT-Tester model checker tool. Furthermore he has assisted in
running the SAL versions of the model for the purpose of comparing to the RT-
Tester tool. During the project, he has also assisted in guidance in cooperation
with Anne Elisabeth Haxthausen.
I would likewise thank Chris Georg for giving an introduction to the RSL-SAL
tool and also for providing support for the tool by e-mail.
I will also thank the SAL team and especially Bruno Dutertre for answering
e-mail according to a problem of accessing arrays in SAL.
I also acknowledge Andreas Foldager, master student at DTU, who has been
writing his master thesis in the same subject, and with whom I have had dis-
cussions according to the subject during the project period.

Chapter 1
Introduction
For this project a specication language for state based models has been de-
veloped, and in particularly it supports writing generic models for the future
danish interlocking system. The language developed is based on the RSL-SAL
language, and then this language has been extended in order to support, what
we need for specifying a generic model for the future danish interlocking systems.
We want to specify a generic model for future danish interlocking systems, so
that we can use formal model checking in order check that safety properties
are valid in order make sure derailment and collision does not occur for the
future system. Safety is of high importance of such systems. By specifying a
generic model for such systems and by applying a model checker to the model,
we have an automated way of checking the safety of such systems. As the model
is generic, it is not dependent on the specic system, meaning it can be used
to check dierent interlocking systems that describes dierent actual physical
stations in Denmark.
By dening a language including type checker to specify such generic model, the
workload of specifying and perhaps changing the model decreases, as the type
checker can nd errors in the specication early on, before running the model
checking. By applying a translator that can translate from this specication
language into the internal language of model checking tool, we give the user the
possibility to do early checks of his model using the type checker, and then if
==PAGE:18==
2
Introduction
the type checker succeeds, he can check his model using the model checker tool.
It is assumed that the reader of this report has basic knowledge of model check-
ing and transition systems. Furthermore it is assumed that the reader has a
basic knowledge of logic and logical expressions.
1.1
Overview of Chapters
In chapter 2, a description is given of RSL-SAL. This chapter includes a small
description of what SAL can be used for, and what Gentle is, as the RSL-SAL
tool is based on Gentle, and as RSL-SAL includes a translator into SAL.
Then in chapter 3, we look at what constructs, we need our language to support,
and how these constructs can be handled in RSL-SAL. This is based on, what
we need in order to specify a generic model for the future danish interlocking
systems. Then it is discussed, how this can be handled using RSL-SAL, and
how the language could be extended in order to support this in a better way.
In chapter 4, we describe extensions to the concrete syntax of the language, and
how the type checker handles these extensions. The extension of the concrete
syntax is described by grammar rules, while the extension of the type checker is
described by inference rules. Furthermore the meaning of these new expressions
is described, which is done by giving a corresponding expression in SAL. The
extensions of the language is based on version 2.6 of RSL-SAL.
How these extensions of the RSL-SAL language is implemented is described in
chapter 5. It is described, how the extensions to grammar and type checker are
implemented, and how the extensions is implemented for the RSL-SAL to SAL
translator.
Chapter 6 address some bugs and problems found in the original RSL-SAL tool.
These problems are illustrated by an example specication, and a solution to
the problem is given. These problems are solved in the extended tool.
We have described a small example illustrating the use of the tool, and especially
the use of arrays in chapter 7.
A generic model for future danish interlocking systems is given in chapter 8.
Here is a description of the generic model, and there is a discussion of the
model. Furthermore it is described that this generic model can be instantiated
by example systems, in order to translate into SAL and perform model checking.
==PAGE:19==

For this project, the generic model has been instantiated by 5 dierent examples.
The SAL model checker tool has been used to verify the generic model for the 5

==PAGE:20==
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
RSL-SAL
RSL-SAL is a specication language, where one can use RSL to specify a tran-
sition system. The tool includes a type checker for the specication, and also
a translator into SAL. If the type checker succeeds for the specication, it can
be translated into SAL by the tool. SAL can then be used to model check the
specication. The RSL-SAL tool is implemented using Gentle[Sch05][Sch].
This chapter rst gives an overall description of what can be done using SAL.
Furthermore the chapter gives a description of Gentle, as the RSL-SAL tool is
implemented using Gentle.
2.1
SAL
In SAL one can set up a number of transitions and a number of assertions.
Then the model checker will evaluate through the transitions and check if the
assertions hold[LdMS03][Dut].
The SAL tool includes a wellformedness checker. This means if one has a speci-
cation in SAL, then one can check the wellformedness of the specication using
the sal-wfc command. Furthermore one can use SAL to perform a global model
check, which can be done using the command sal-smc. For this case, the model
==PAGE:22==
6
RSL-SAL
is evaluated based on initial values of the variables, and according to transitions,
these variables can be given new values. Another approach is to use bounded
model checking using induction, which can be done using the sal-bmc command.
For this case, a property must be an input argument. Then the tool will by in-
duction prove that if the property hold in some state, then the property will
also hold in all the following states according to the transitions.
In SAl one can specify transitions for a system. For instance if one has the
variables a and b, and these are both booleans, and we also have a type Subtype
which could be a nite subtype of integer and a function f that takes in an
element of type Subtype and gives a boolean, then one can write a transition
system in the following way:
TRANSITION
[
a AND b −−> a ' = FALSE; b ' = FALSE
[ ]
( [ ]
( i :
Subtype )
f ( i ) −−> a ' = TRUE )
]
END;
Here we have two transitions, where the second one is a comprehended transi-
tion. Looking at the rst transition, we check if both a and b are true, and if
his is the case, we set both a and b to false. In the second transition, we iterate
through all values in the type Subtype, and if the function f evaluates to true
for a value, then we set a to true.
We see that it is possible to dene functions that can take input parameters
and return a value of some type. Furthermore we see that we can have compre-
hended transitions. If we have a type describing a set of elements, then these
comprehended transitions can be used to iterate through all these elements.
Using such comprehended transition requires that the type one iterates through
has a nite set of elements.
For checking some assertion, we can write up assertions in the following way:
ass1
: THEOREM System |−G( a ) ;
Here ass1 represents an identier for the assertion. When a transition system is
specied it is also given a name. The assertion shown above is for a transition
system called System. This assertion says that the variable a is always true in
the transition system System.
==PAGE:23==
2.2 Gentle
7
We see that in SAL, we can specify models, where for the model we can have
some functions, and we can also have constant values.
Furthermore we can
have a set of variables and transitions. In these transitions, we can access the
variables, constant values and functions, and assign new value to the variables.
Lastly, we have a number of assertion, which the model checker can check the
validity of.
2.2
Gentle
The Gentle tool can be used to compile high-level specications. In Gentle we
have some dierent keywords[Sch05][Sch]:
module
The module keyword is used to specify the module. For instance, one
can have an abstract syntax of a specication dened in a le, and one might
want to call this module ast, then the le should begin with the line:
' module '
ast
The module keyword can only be used once in a le, and this keyword needs to
be specied at the very top of the le.
use
Multiple modules can be used to organize and structure the code, so that
dierent modules contains dierent appropriate actions, types etc. For instance,
one can have specied an abstract syntax in a module called ast, and maybe
in another module, one wants to use the specications of this module. Then one
can use the following:
' use '
ast
in order to be able to use the content of the module ast. For a module, the
use keyword can only be used once in a module, but several modules can be
referenced by using the use keyword once. Each module should then just be
separated by a comma (,) or by a space. Furthermore the use keyword must be
used before types, nonterms, actions, conditions or tables are specied in the
le.
export
Furthermore if one has the module ast specied, and this contains
types or actions or other denitions that might be used in other les that uses
==PAGE:24==
8
RSL-SAL
the ast module, then one need to specify in the ast module that these types,
actions etc are exported. Meaning if one has dened the type VALUE_EXPR
in a module, and this is used by other modules, then the module containing the
denition of VALUE_EXPR must contain the following
' export ' VALUE_EXPR
A le can only contain the export keyword once, but then several elements
can be referenced, where each element is separated by a comma (,) or a space.
Like for the use keyword, export must appear before types, nonterms, actions,
conditions or tables are specied.
type
This can be used to dene dierent types, which can be used in abstract
syntax. Each type can then have dierent functors and constants. For instance
we can have a type for describing value expressions, and for this we can have a
functor for inx operations:
' type ' VALUE_EXPR
val_infix
( pos
:
POS,
l e f t
:
VALUE_EXPR
op
:
OP
right
:
VALUE_EXPR)
' type ' OP
plus ,
minus
Here the functor val_inx describes some inx operator between two value
expressions. For the example OP is also dened as a type, and here a constant
could for instance represent the plus-operator. The pos is used to describe the
position of the expression, which can be used for printing error messages. A
module can contain several types. The POS type is built-in for Gentle.
nonterm
Nonterminals are used to describe reading of concrete syntax. A
nonterminal cannot take any arguments as input, but it can give output. A
nonterm consists of a set of rules, which can be applied.
An example of a
nonterm could be
' nonterm '
value_infix_expr ( −> VALUE_EXPR)
' rule '
value_infix_expr(−> val_infix (P,
Left , Op,
Right ))
value_expr(−> Left )
infix_op(−> Op)

2.2 Gentle
9
value_expr(−> Right )
@(−> P)
' nonterm '
infix_op(−> OP)
' rule '
infix_op(−> plus )
"+"
' rule '
infix_op(−> minus )
"−"
The nonterm inx_op can be used to read either the plus or minus opera-
tor, which it can do if it see the + or - in the concrete syntax.
Then
value_inx_expr will be successful, if it can read a value expression then some
inx operator and lastly another value expression. According to the above one
should specify a nonterm value_expr for reading a value expression. Such non-
terminals can be set to read a concrete syntax and generate an abstract syntax
three. We also see in the above that we read the position of the inx value
expression. This use of position is built-in for Gentle. If for instance a type
checker fails for this expression, one can print the position of the expression to
the user. A module can contain several nonterms.
condition
A condition can take some input, but cannot give a output. A con-
dition also consists of a number of rules. For instance one can create condition
checking that the inx operator is plus:
' condition '
is_plus (OP)
' rule '
is_plus ( plus )
This condition only succeeds if the operator it takes in is plus.
action
An action can take in some input and give some output.
Let us
for instance say, we translate a specication into another language.
Let us
say, we have dened an abstract and concrete syntax according to the rules
above, and now we have dened a type LANG2_VALUE_EXPR with the func-
tor lang2_inx_op(left:
LANG2_VALUE_EXPR, op:
LANG2_OP, right:
LANG2_VALUE_EXPR). Let us say that this language LANG2 does not sup-
port minus operator, but supports plus, one can have the following action in
order to transform an expression in the original abstract syntax into an expres-
sion of the alternative abstract syntax:
' action '
original_to_alt_val_expr (VALUE_EXPR −>
LANG2_VALUE_EXPR)

RSL-SAL

original_to_alt_val_expr ( Right −> RightRes )
It is assumed that the original_to_alt_val_expr action contains further rules
for handling other possible value expressions, like it is assumed that origi-
nal_to_alt_op is an action that can convert an element of type OP into LANG2_OP.
A module can contain several actions.
rule
The rule keyword is used to dene dierent rules for nonterms, actions
and conditions. Gentle will start from the top of a nonterm/action/condition,
and try applying the rules until it nds a successful rule.
An action/condition/nonterm can contain several rules.
root
The root keyword is used to instantiate a compilation. For instance if
one uses a set of nonterms, actions and conditions to describe a way to read and
type check a concrete syntax, then outermost, we should a have a root, which
should be able to this by applying other nonterms/actions/conditions .
table
The keyword table can be used to dene tables. For instance one can
dene a Variable_id that can be used to hold dierent variables. In the example
below, we have a table for ids of variables, where each variable contains an ID,
a TYPE_EXPR describing the type of the variable and an INITIALISATION,
describing the initial value of the variable. In the example below, it is assumed,
we have ID, TYPE_EXPR and INITIALISATION dened as types.
' table '
Varaible_id
( Ident : ID ,
Type :TYPE_EXPR,
I n i t : INITIALISATION)
==PAGE:27==
2.3 RSL
11
2.3
RSL
RSL is a specication language[Geo08], one can use to specify a system. This
language supports the use of transitions and creation of a transition system.
Likewise one can specify assertions to check if certain properties hold throughout
the evaluation of the system[CG06].
2.4
RSL-SAL
The RSL-SAL tool includes a translator from RSL-SAL to SAL, so that one can
specify a transition system in RSL-SAL, and check the assertions using SAL.
This translator generates three versions, a non-cc-version and two cc-versions
(a simle version and a normal version). The cc-versions have the purpose of
checking condence conditions. The type checker of the RSL-SAL tool will only
check expressions according to maximal type of variables and expressions.
This tool uses Gentle in order to translate a specication from RSL-SAL into
SAL.
2.4.1
CC-version
The translator generates the cc-version, where all types are translated into a
lifted type. All variables are of such lifted types, so each variable can either be
a value describing the correct value of the variable or it can have a nav-value
(nav stands for not-a-value). If a variable is given a nav-value, it means that at
some point an illegal value is assigned to the variable.
This way for the cc-version all expressions assigning a value to a variable are
translated into an expression, where the value rstly is checked to be in the
correct range. For instance if one has a subtype called Subint and a variable a
of type Subint, and we have he expression:
a = e
where a is the variable a and e is some expression, then this is translated into:
a = i f
is_Subint ( e ) then e
e l s e
nav
==PAGE:28==
12
RSL-SAL
where is_Subint is some expression taking in the lifted type of the maximal
type of Subint as an argument and returning a boolean indicating whether the
value of e is inside the type Subint. Here the is_Subint will take in the lifted
type of integer, and if the input is a nav-value or the input represent an integer
that is not in the subset described by the Subint set, then false is returned.
The translator also generates an assertion, which check that it is always true
that no variables has a nav-value.
This way the condence conditions are checked by running the SAL model-
checker for the generated cc-version. And if any variable at any point is given
an illegal value (represented by a nav-value), then the assertion will be invalid
and a counter example is given to the user.
2.4.2
Non-cc-version
If the cc-version terminates with valid assertions, then one can run the non-cc-
version which does the model-checking and checks the assertions dened by the
user in RSL. If one runs the non-cc-version without checking the specication
according to the cc-version, one risk getting a runtime error from SAL. In this
case SAL will give a message telling at what line in the generated SAL code
that the error occurred. However using this method, the user is not shown a
counter example leading to the illegal expression. And likewise then the user
needs to look at the generated SAL code, which can be avoided by running the
cc-version.
2.4.3
Finite types
The translator generates a system, where all types are nite.
Therefore in-
tegers and natural are translated into nite type. The user can in the RSL-
specication, specify global minimum and maximums of integer and natural.
This is done by dening the following values:
value
IntHigh :
Int = 10 , IntLow :
Int = −5, NatHigh :
Int =
20
If this is not explicitly set by the user in RSL, the translator will set 4 as the
global maximum for integers and naturals, while −4 is set as the global minimum
for integers.
==PAGE:29==
2.4 RSL-SAL
13
2.4.4
Translating
When translating and running a model-check in SAL, one should start by choose
type check the RSL specication. If this succeeds, he should translate the RSL
specication into SAL. If the type checking is successful, then the tool will re-
solve the abstract syntax three generated by the type checker. If the resolver
terminates correctly, it will translate into SAL. This will result in three SAL-
specications (non-cc, cc and cc-simple). After this one should use the SAL
wellformedness-checker to check the well-formedness of the translated specica-
tions. After this running the cc-version can ensure that condence conditions
are not broken.
Lastly the non-cc-version can be model-checked using SAL,
according to user-specied assertions.
==PAGE:30==
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 3
Challenges and
Considerations
In this chapter, the dierent challenges in extending the current RSL tool to deal
with our needs according to the model, are discussed. We have the following as
main challenges:
• Priorities of transitions
• Quantied variables
• Bit operators
This is something that is used in our generic model for future interlocking sys-
tems. In this chapter, we look at some of the things, we would like to support,
and how this is possible in RSL-SAL. The chapter also looks at how the tool
could be extended in order to specify such generic model.
We base the needs of the specication language according to a concrete generic
model for future danish interlocking system, however we try to make the solution
as general as possible.
==PAGE:32==
16
Challenges and Considerations
3.1
Priorities
For the concrete model worked with in this paper, we have 4 dierent sets
of transitions: Φd, Φl, Φϵ and Φτ.
These sets of transitions are ordered in
priorities, so that transitions in Φl should always be chosen instead of transitions
in Φϵ if possible, and that transitions of Φϵ should always be chosen before
transitions in Φτ. However transitions of Φd can be chosen independently of
whether transitions are available in the other sets.
This means that when we have two sets of transitions they should either be
prioritized so that transitions in one of the sets are chosen before the other. Or
otherwise the two sets should be prioritized equally, meaning transitions from
either set can be chosen.
For now in RSL, it is not possible to dene priority-relations between transitions.
We can initially dene a bunch of transitions, and we cannot explicitly dene
which should be chosen.
Each transition have some guards describing what
should hold in order for the transition can be chosen.
For our specic example, we want to be able to specify 4 dierent types of pri-
orities, A, B, C and D, where priority B is higher than C, and C is higher
than D. However the A priority is a special priority, meaning that these transi-
tions should be independent of all other transitions. Eg. if there is a transition
available with priority A, one available with priority B, and one available with
priority C, either the one with priority A or priority B should be chosen. If
we have a transition with priority A available and a transition with priority C
available, and no transitions with priority B available, then either the transition
with priority A or the one with priority C is chosen. Transitions with prior-
ity A should not be prioritized above transitions with priority C, even though
transitions with priority A and transitions with priority B should be prioritized
equally, and transitions with priority B are prioritized above transitions with
priority C.
We can write:
B >p C ∧A =p B ∧A =p C.
where =p means equal priority, and >p means greater priority. Generally we
need to be able to specify these two operators between transitions and between
sets of transitions.
==PAGE:33==
3.1 Priorities
17
3.1.1
Manually alter Guards
A simple and naive way of handling this problem could be to manually extend
the guards of the transitions so that priorities are hold.
Eg. one could have the following two transitions, Trans1 and Trans2.
Trans1:
a ∧b ⇒a′ = false
Trans2:
c ∧d ⇒c′ = false
Let us say, we want that Trans1 has a higher priority that Trans2, then we
modify the specication to the following.
Trans1:
a ∧b ⇒a′ = false
Trans2:
¬(a ∧b) ∧c ∧d ⇒c′ = false
Now in order for Trans1 to be chosen a and b must hold, which means that the
guard of Trans2 does not hold. Meaning if Trans1 can be chosen, then Trans2
will not be chosen. Now let us say, we have a third transition Trans3, and this
transition should be prioritized equally to both Trans1 and Trans2, then we
just do nothing with the guards. We do not modify the guard of Trans3, and
we do no further modications of the guards of Trans1 and Trans2.
For this method, we can do all kind of priority relations between transitions.
We can handle the demands of the four sets, Φd, Φl, Φϵ and Φτ. Furthermore
let us say that for the set of Φϵ, we have three subsets Φϵ1, Φϵ2 and Φϵ3. And
we want Φϵ2 to have higher priority compared Φϵ3, but we want equal priority
between Φϵ1 and Φϵ2 and between Φϵ1 and Φϵ3. This is also possible with this
approach, even though it is not required for our concrete generic model.
However this approach is very time-consuming, error-prone and not very user-
friendly, as the user himself have to convert the guards of all his transitions
manually in order to comply with his priorities. Furthermore small errors can
be dicult to see. One might forget to specify a guard correctly, meaning his
priorities are not as intended, which he might never notice, as the model will
still run.
3.1.1.1
Two alternatives
Let us say, we have the following two sets, T1 and T2 , of transitions, where the
transitions in T1 should be prioritized above T2.
==PAGE:34==
18
Challenges and Considerations
T1 =
a1
⇒
...
...
an
⇒
...
T2 =
b1
⇒
...
...
bm
⇒
...
These can either be change to the set T of transitions
T =
a1
⇒
...
...
an
⇒
...
¬(a1 ∨... ∨an) ∧b1
⇒
...
...
¬(a1 ∨... ∨an) ∧bm
⇒
...
Otherwise one can introduce a helping variable, for instance called possible,
which initially is set to true, and create the following transition set T ′:
T ′ =
a1
⇒
...
...
an
⇒
...
¬(a1 ∨... ∨an)
⇒
possible = false
¬possible ∧b1
⇒
possible = true, ...
...
¬possible ∧bm
⇒
possible = true, ...
The rst method is more naive, and using the second method should increase
performance. For the rst method, then each time, a new transition is chosen,
then the expressions a1 ∨... ∨an worst-case will be evaluated m times extra,
where for the second method, this is only evaluated one time extra every time
a transition is chosen.
However for the second method, we have to do one
transition-step setting possible to false, and then a another step to choose a
transition with lower priority, but this overhead is still constant.
Using the second approach the overhead of transition-steps is dependent on the
number of priority layers in the specication.
==PAGE:35==
3.1 Priorities
19
3.1.2
Explicit Priorities
Another solution is to explicitly for each transition give it a priority represented
by an integer. This way 1 could mean higher priority than 2 etc. This way we
can specify an arbitrary number of priorities, where all transitions are prioritized
compared to each other. This approach solves our problem with the B, C and D
priorities. Then for handling the A priority, we could decide that 0 is a special
priority, meaning the transition is independent of all other priorities. This solves
what is required by our specic example.
Let us say, we have three sets set1, set2 and set3, and that set1 =p set2,
set1 =p set3 and set2 >p set3, we can solve this by giving the transitions in
set1 priority 0, the transitions in set2 priority 1 and the transitions in set3
priority 2.
But let us say, in set2 we have three subsets of transitions, set2A, set2B and
set2C, where set2A =p set2B, set2A =p set2C and set2B >p set2C. This we
can not handle with this approach. For handling this we need a second layer of
priority for the transitions, so that transitions in set set2A should have priority
1 in the rst layer and priority 0 in second layer. This way for multiple layers,
we need to specify multiple priorities for each transition, which does not seem
like a neat solution.
So even though this solves the problem for the specic example, this does not
seem to be a general approach. However an advantage of this approach is that,
it does not matter in which order we write the transitions.
The specic example can be specied using this approach having just one layer
of priorities, however for a general solution to the problem at hand, one would
need to introduce multiple layers of priorities.
3.1.3
Transitional Operators
A third solution could be to introduce operations between transitions. For now
in RSL, transitions are separated by [=], meaning they have equal priority.
We could introduce another symbol [>], meaning the transitions on the left
side have greater priority compared to the transitions on the right side. So let
us look at an example where we have the sets:
==PAGE:36==
20
Challenges and Considerations
set1
=
{trans1, trans2}
set2
=
{trans3, trans4, trans5, trans6, trans7}
set2A
=
{trans3}
set2B
=
{trans4}
set2C
=
{trans5, trans6, trans7}
set3
=
{trans8}
set4
=
{trans9, trans10}
And we want the sets to have have the following priorities:
set1
=p
set2
set1
=p

set1
=p
set4

>p
set3
set3

)
Here we have a general way of specifying the priority between transitions. Even
though this method requires the user to user to write up the transitions in order
related to their priority level, the user can freely specify how each transition
should be prioritized to other transitions.
==PAGE:37==
3.1 Priorities
21
3.1.4
If-Then-Else
Another alternative is to introduce if-then-else sentences outside transitions.
This is for instance supported by the RT-Tester model checker tool.
An if-
then-else approach would mean that the user should specify a conditions, and
if the condition holds, then choose from a certain set of transitions, and if the
condition does not hold, then choose from some other set of transitions.
Everything you specify using the method with a transitional operator, described
in section 3.1.3, you will also be able to specify using this If-Then-Else method.
However sometimes it is not that easy to do this.
If we have the following
transitions
(
a1 ==> a1 ' = f a l s e
[=]
. . .
[=]
an ==> an ' = f a l s e
)
[ >]
(
b1 ==> b1 ' = f a l s e
[=]
. . .
[=]
bm ==> bm' = f a l s e
)
this could be written in the following using If-Then-Else syntax:
i f
( a1 \/
. . .
\/ an )
{
a1 ==> a1 ' = f a l s e
[=]
. . .
[=]
an ==> an ' = f a l s e
}
e l s e
{
b1 ==> b1 ' = f a l s e
[=]
. . .
[=]
bm ==> bm' = f a l s e
}
We see that the user needs to manually write the condition of the if sentence as
the disjunction of guards.
Likewise it is generally possible to write any expression of If-Then-Else syntax
into a specication using syntax from Section 3.1.3, where we use [>]. Take
for instance the general case.
==PAGE:38==
22
Challenges and Considerations
i f ( c ) {
a1 ==> a1 ' = f a l s e
[=]
. . .
[=]
an ==> an ' = f a l s e
}
e l s e
{
b1 ==> b1 ' = f a l s e
[=]
. . .
[=]
bm ==> bm' = f a l s e
}
Then we can rewrite it as follow:
(
[ newTrans1 ]
c /\ chooseNewTrans ==>
chooseTrans1 ' = true /\
chooseTrans2 ' = f a l s e
/\
chooseTransNewTrans ' = f a l s e
[=]
[ newTrans2 ]
~c /\ chooseNewTrans ==>
chooseTrans1 ' = f a l s e
/\
chooseTrans2 ' = true /\
chooseNewTrans ' = f a l s e
)
[ >]
(
a1 /\ chooseTrans1 ==>
a1'= f a l s e
/\ chooseNewTrans ' = true
[=]
. . .
[=]
an /\ chooseTrans1 ==>
an'= f a l s e
/\ chooseNewTrans ' = true
[=]
b1 /\ chooseTrans2 ==>
b1'= f a l s e
/\ chooseNewTrans ' = true
[=]
. . .
[=]
bm /\ chooseTrans2 ==>
bm'= f a l s e
/\ chooseNewTrans ' = true
)
Here we need to specify new transitions newTrans1 and newTrans2 and new
variables chooseNewTrans, chooseTrans1 and chooseTrans2. And we need
to make sure that the variable chooseNewTrans is set to true always, when
==PAGE:39==
3.1 Priorities
23
we choose one of the original transitions. So we see that the general case of an
if-sentence can be specied using the method from Section 3.1.3, but it is not
very friendly and not super intuitive.
Generally these are two dierent things, and they are appropriate for dierent
matters. Ona can imagine a case, where we have some independent variable,
which is not used by the guards of any of the transitions. However dependent
on the value of this variable, the system should choose a transition from two
dierent sets of transitions. For instance, we could have a variable describing a
point in time, and dependent on this variable, then dierent transitions should
be possible to choose. However this is something dierent for what we want.
3.1.5
Multiple Transition System
Another approach one could imagine is having multiple transition systems, and
then combining these in some way. However this can correspond to the dierent
sets of transitions we have. A dierence for this is that for instance we declare
all variables the same place, even though some might only be used by specic
sets of the transitions. There is not a need for this in this project, as long as we
can divide the transitions up into sets.
If we dene multiple transition systems, we still need a way to prioritize the
transition systems.
3.1.6
Conclusion
We have decided to go with the method of transitional operator [>] from
Section 3.1.3. The manual method from Section 3.1.1 is just a way one can do
the requirement using the current RSL, and this is not a good solution for the
problem, as it is too error-prone and time-consuming. That method is the most
naive approach.
The approach described in Section 3.1.2, using explicit specication of the prior-
ity in the transitions, is a solution for the specic problem. It is easy and simple
to just use an integer describing the priority of the transition. However the
method described in Section 3.1.3 is more general, where we can have multiple
layers of priorities.
Lastly the method of If-Then-Else from Section 3.1.4 also have some advantages
compared to the method of the new transitional operator from Section 3.1.3.
==PAGE:40==
24
Challenges and Considerations
If we for a set of transitions, all transitions have the same sub expression in
the guard, then using the If-Then-Else method, we can just specify this as a
condition of the if-sentence. If we want the same in the other approach, we
need to explicitly specify this in the guards of the transitions. However for our
example we just want to specify priorities which should always be the same. If
this is what one wants, then the If-Then-Else approach could be error-prone,
as then the user himself need to create the disjunction of guards in a set of
transitions. Furthermore one might want to translate into a language that does
not supports If-Then-Else statements. However then one could still translate
so that the condition of the if-sentence becomes a part of the guards of the
transitions.
Likewise with the hard priorities as in the transitional operator method, one
can always translate into another language by alternating the guards. Then the
translator should just translate according to the manual method described in
Section 3.1.1.
We see that the general case of an if-sentence is not super intuitive to write using
the [>] and [=] operators, however likewise hard priorities are more intuitive
to write using [>] and [=] compared to If-Then-Else. As the requirement for
this is hard priorities, the solution using [>] is chosen.
There might be other models, where an If-Then-Else statement is more appro-
priate, but for this problem we have used the transitional operators from Section
3.1.3.
3.2
Variables
For the concrete problem, we have dierent variables for dierent entities. For
instance we can have a Point entity, and then for each point we can have dier-
ent variables. Let us for instance say, for each Point, we have the three variable
A, B and C. Let us for example say that these three variables are all inte-
gers. Furthermore as the model is generic, the specication should work for an
arbitrary number of Points.
We have dierent discussions for this.
We have a discussion of how we collect the dierent variables associated to for
instance Point. Here a solution could be to use a record type describing a point,
while another could be to create three dierent variables, where one variable
is a map from point id into a value describing the eld A, and likewise maps
==PAGE:41==
3.2 Variables
25
describing the value of the B-eld and C-eld of the dierent points.
Secondly we want our model to be generic, which means that the model should
apply for dierent number of points. Examples for solving this could be to for
instance use a map, from some point id to a point.
Thirdly we have a discussion of how we should describe the id of the points.
Whether this should be described as a subtype or using variant types. This is
described in section 3.2.3.
Fourthly and lastly we also discuss how we describe the types that should repre-
sent the elds of the point, meaning how we want to describe ranged variables.
3.2.1
Collection of Fields
This section discusses, how one could collect the dierent elds associated to
point.
3.2.1.1
Records
One solution is to specify a record type to represent the points. This can be
done in RSL by the statement:
type Point
: :
A:
Int
B:
Int
C:
Int
Now let us say we for instance have a variable of type Point called p. Now let
us say we have a transition that says for a point that if A is 3, then A is set to
1. This we can specify in RSL using the following.
[ Trans1 ]
A(p) = 3 ==> p ' = mk_Point (1 ,B(p ) ,C(p ))
We see here that we have functions for accessing the dierent elds of the record
type, but if we want to change the value for a single eld, then we need to use
the mk_Point to create a new instance, and we need to pass values for all the
elds.
==PAGE:42==
26
Challenges and Considerations
If one wanted to remove some of the redundancy of this expression one might
want to write the same statement like the following
[ Trans1 ]
A(p) = 3 ==> A(p ' ) = 1
or
[ Trans1 ]
A(p) = 3 ==> p ' .A = 1
However this is not supported in RSL. For doing this one would need to extend
the syntax of RSL.
The advantage of this approach is that we have all variables attached to the
same type collected somewhere. We have all variables for the points collected
in the same record. However we also see that unless the syntax is changed, then
it is not nice to change the value of just one variable in a record. Especially
if the record contains many dierent variables, the user would have to write a
rather long and redundant expression just to change one value. A solution to
this could be to change the syntax.
For instance, we might want that the expression:
p ' .A = 1
is read and stored in the abstract syntax as it was:
mk_Point (1 ,B(p ) ,C(p ))
However this is not so simple, as by looking at just the simple expression, we
cannot just turn it into the more complex expression, as we miss some informa-
tion about the other elds of the Point record.
We see here that using records is a way to gather all the variables related to
points the same spot.
3.2.1.2
Multiple Variables
An alternative to using the record type could be to use a number of variables.
Then we would in this case have three variables, where one represent A, one
represent B and one represent C, instead of having these variables gathered in a
==PAGE:43==
3.2 Variables
27
record type. For instance one could have three maps, where one map describes
the value of A for dierent points, and likewise a map that describes values for B
and one for C. If one chooses this approach and one wants to update the value
of one variable, then one does not get the overhead as one gets using records.
This approach is also discussed in the coming sections.
3.2.1.3
Product
One might also think of using Product type for this, however for this type, we do
not have a way of just accessing the value of one of the elds, and we cannot just
assign a new value to just one of the elds of the type. This is not appropriate
for representing this.
3.2.1.4
Array or Map
Another alternative is to use a map for representing a single point. Then the map
should contain an element describing the value of A, and elements for describing
the value of B and C. Likewise one can use an array. How to use maps and
arrays is described in section 3.2.2, especially in sections 3.2.2.1 and 3.2.2.3. The
dierence for using maps or arrays here is that we have xed size domain which
is not dependent on network for the interlocking. However this method requires
that we must specify a type for describing domain of the map/array, meaning
we must dene a type for describing elds for the Point type. This approach
might be overkill.
3.2.2
Generic Variables
As the model should be generic, we do not know, how many points each speci-
cation contains. Therefore we need some way to describe all the points. This
is discussed in this section.
3.2.2.1
Maps
For storing multiple points, we could use maps. Whether one chooses to use
record type or multiple variables as described in Section 3.2.1.1 to represent
a single point, we still need a way to represent multiple points. This section
==PAGE:44==
28
Challenges and Considerations
discusses how maps can be used, and for the section it is presumed that we do
not use the record type to describe a single point.
For each variable, we have a map that maps from an identier of a point into
the value of the variable.
That means for the example with the Point type
containing three variables, we would specify like the following in RSL
type
PointA = Int −m−> Int ,
PointB = Int −m−> Int ,
PointC = Int −m−> Int
We instantiate one variable of each of these types. Eg. we instantiate a variable
pa to be of type PointA. This variable pa represents the value of A for all points.
Now let us say, we have a transition that checks if the value of A is 3, then we
set the value to 1. Let us say we are checking for the point with id 1. Then this
can be specied like this:
[ Trans1 ]
pa (1) = 3 ==> pa ' = pa
! !
[1 +> 1]
For this solution, we only need to pass the value, we want to change. However
we still have some redundancies. Eg. another way which is simpler to write the
same expression could be:
[ Trans1 ]
pa (1) = 3 ==> pa ' ( 1 ) = 1
This is not supported in RSL at the moment. However it could be a possibility
to extend the syntax of RSL to handle this.
For this approach, we do not have all variables for eg Point collected the same
place. This might not seem intuitive. However compared to the Record type,
when changing the value of just one variable, we do not need to explicitly rewrite
the value of all other variables in the same object.
One downside for this approach might be that it is not easy to check that if we
have specied the variable A for the point p, then in the current RSL, we cannot
be certain that B is also specied for the point, as this is stored in two dierent
maps, and the maps do not necessarily have the same domains. Even though
they have the same domain type, we might not be certain that all elements of
this domain type is represented in the domain of the maps.
==PAGE:45==
3.2 Variables
29
Also if we have a map pa, one could write the expression
[ TransX ]
true ==> pa = [ ]
where one sets the map pa to be empty. This is not desired as the domains of
these maps should always correspond exactly to the points in the model. We
might run into a case, where pa(1) is not dened.
So if one wanted to use maps, one could specify the types in the following way:
type
PointA = {|m :
Int −m−> Int :−dom m = all_points |} ,
PointB = {|m :
Int −m−> Int :−dom m = all_points |} ,
PointC = {|m :
Int −m−> Int :−dom m = all_points |}
allpoints is a set representing all the identiers of the points. Here we spec-
ify that the domain of the maps should always be equal to the set containing
identiers of all the points.
Likewise instead of using Int as input for the maps, one could specify a subtype,
which is all integers in the set allpoints. One would still need to make this
explicit check of the domain of the maps.
If one wanted to use record types, then we could still use a map to represent all
the points, but in this case, we just have a single map going from an identier
of a point into a variable of record type representing the point. Then here, we
would not worry that all the maps describing dierent variables should have the
same domain, however the user could still specify the map to be empty, which
is not desirable.
3.2.2.2
Total functions
An alternative to using maps is using total functions. For total functions, we
do not need to worry that a user might set the domain to be the empty set.
Here we just need to specify for each function what the possible input-values
are and then we do not need to worry that the domain for the functions change.
If we use the approach of not using record type but using multiple variables
as described in section 3.2.1.2, then we just have three total functions, one for
describing A, one for B and one for describing C. And then we specify these
three functions to have the same input values.
==PAGE:46==
30
Challenges and Considerations
So let us say, we do not use record types, then this approach might be better,
as the user will not be able to change the domains of the dierent functions.
If we do not use record types, then one could specify the three total functions
using the following
type
PointA = Int −> Int ,
PointB = Int −> Int ,
PointC = Int −> Int
Now let us say that one have instantiated a total function of type PointA, and
this variable is called pa. Then lets say, we have a transition that checks if the
value of A is 3, then we set the value to 1. Let us say we are checking for the
point with id 1. Then this can be specied like this:
[ Trans1 ]
pa (1) = 3 ==> pa ' = −\ i
:
Int :−
i f
i=1 then 1
e l s e
pa ( i )
This is supported in the current RSL, but the specication is not very nice. So
we might want to change the syntax so that the following could be supported:
[ Trans1 ]
pa (1) = 3 ==> pa ' ( 1 ) = 1
In order to do this, one has to be aware that one would need to somehow know
the type of the input of the function as this is specically specied in the rst
example. Here we have the problem that in the abstract syntax representing
the lambda expression in the above, we also store the type of i. In the simplied
expression, we do not have the type of i, and therefore we cannot directly expand
the simple statement into the the more complex statement. For this we need to
look through the declaration of pa.
We see here that Int is used as input for the functions in the example, however
one could specify a subtype to represent that the possible input-values should
eg. be in the subset {1..10} if we have 10 points. This is discussed in Section
3.2.3
3.2.2.3
Arrays
Another alternative to total functions and maps is using arrays. Here we specify
an array of a certain size, where the size is the number of points in the speci-
cation. Again if we do not use record types for describing a single point, but
==PAGE:47==
3.2 Variables
31
use the approach of multiple variables as described in section 3.2.1.2, then we
have multiple arrays, all describing a single thing for each point.
If we do not use record types, this approach has an advantage compared to
using maps, as we cannot change the domain of an array. We start by setting
the size of the array, and since arrays are xed-sized, we know the size of the
array. One should just make sure to specify all three arrays to have the same
size corresponding to the number of points in the specication, and then the
value at index i all represent values for the same point.
If one wanted to use record type for describing a single point, then arrays could
still be used to represent all the points, but then one would just need one array
for describing the points.
Arrays however are not supported in RSL, and one would need to extend the
RSL. One could specify the language so that an array can be declared in the
following way.
A[ p : Int ]
:
Int
B[ p : Int ]
:
Int
C[ p : Int ]
:
Int
Then for accessing the values in the arrays, one could have the following
[ Trans1 ]
A[ 1 ] = 3 ==> A' [ 1 ] = 1
where we check if the value of A for the point with id 1 is equal to 3. Then if
this is true, we set the value of A to 1.
Likewise instead of using Int as index for the arrays, one could use a subtype
to specify that the array should only be described for indexes in the set {0..n}.
This can be done the same way as it can be done for total functions.
If one wanted to use array to collect the variables associated with a single point
as mentioned in section 3.2.1.4, then for this, we would have an array of two
dimensions.
3.2.2.4
Object Arrays
Another approach one should consider is using object arrays which are supported
in RSL. Using such object arrays, we could specify an object array for points,
and then in this object, we can specify the variables.
==PAGE:48==
32
Challenges and Considerations
object
Point [ i
:
Int ] :
c l a s s
variable A :
Int
variable B :
Int
variable C :
Int
end
Now let us say, we have a transition that says that if the value of the variable A
for the point with id 1 is equal to 3, then we set the value of A to 1. This can
be done using object arrays in the following way.
[ Trans1 ]
Point [ 1 ] .A = 3 ==> Point [ 1 ] . A' = 1
However object arrays are not supported in RSL-SAL, and therefore for this
solution, we would need to extend the RSL-SAL to include object arrays.
3.2.3
Point id Type
We see that from section 3.2.2 that we need a type describing the id of points.
This corresponds to the domain type of map, total function or array. In the
examples above, just Int has been used. However this would for eg. the total
functions mean that an output value is described for each integer. Instead one
could introduce a subtype representing the set containing all identiers of points.
If for instance, we have 5 points, one could specify the following subtype:
type PointID = {| i
:
Int :−i
i s i n
{1..5}
|}
Then one could use this subtype as input for the maps or total functions, or use
this as index for the arrays or object arrays.
If one chooses to use such subtype, another decision lies within whether it should
be a subtype of int or of text. As Text is not supported by the RSL-SAL tool,
int is preferred before text.
Another alternative is to specify this as a variant type:
type PointID == p1
|
p2
|
p3
|
p4
|
p5
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Variables
33
3.2.4
Entity Type
For the concrete example at hand, we have integer values which can only be
within a specic range. This can be solved in dierent ways.
3.2.4.1
Specify Min and Max Value of Integers
One solution could be to specify subtypes, which can be done in the following
way in RSL.
a
:
{| i
:
Int :−{ i
i s i n
{0..4}
|}
For doing this, we do not need to change anything in the syntax, as this is
supported.
3.2.4.2
Variant Type
Another solution is to use the variant type. Sometimes we have a variable that
should have a specic value. For instance, we can have a variable called Mode
and then this variable should always have either the value free, locked, marked,
allocating or used. This can be specied using the variant type like the following.
type Mode == f r e e
|
locked
|
marked
|
a l l o c a t i n g
|
used
For this solution, we do not need to change the syntax of RSL, and for the
example provided here, it is easier to read using this approach compared to
specifying this using an integer.
However we might have other cases, where this approach may not be the most
beautiful. For instance, for the concrete example, we have a variable for each
direction in each section, called hto. This variable should never be less than
0 or more than 7. For this variable, we use three bits, where each bit states
something dierent.
One bit species whether the head of a train is in the
section, one bit species whether the tail of a train is in the section, while the
last bit tells whether the section is occupied by a train. For this the variant
type would look something like this.
type HTO
== Free
| OccAndNoHeadAndNoTail |
TailAndNoHeadAndNotOcc
|
. . .
|
OccWithHeadAndTail
==PAGE:50==
34
Challenges and Considerations
We have dierent variants to describe for instance no train is at the section, or
the section is occupied without having a head or a tail of a train inside it. As we
want to describe al cases for whether the three bits are set, we get some strange
invariants, for instance TailAndNoHeadAndNoOcc. This represents the case
where the tail of a train is in the section but the section is not occupied. This
means something is wrong.
This discussion of the hto variable also relate to the discussion of section 3.3.
3.2.5
Conclusion
We see that we have multiple discussions here. There is a matter of how, we
collect and specify variables attached to the same type, in this example the
variables A, B and C of Point.
And there is the matter of how we specify
multiple points for a concrete model. However these two discussions overlap.
One can use record types to describe eg.
points, which consists of multiple
variables.
For this solution, we get all variables that are relevant for points
collected in the same place. However we have some redundancy when writing
the expressions for changing the value of the variables.
It is heavy to write
expressions updating the value of just one eld of the record. Alternatively an
array or map can be used to collect the elds of a point. This way for each point
we have an array/map for each point, however then we need to introduce a new
type for describing the domain of the map/array. The simple solution to this is
to just create multiple variable describing the dierent elds of a point.
We also need a way to represent the dierent points, and as this is a generic
model, this can be an arbitrary number of elements. For describing the dierent
points, maps, total functions, object arrays and arrays have been considered.
As it is possible to change the domain of maps, it might be more robust to use
total functions as one cannot change the domain of these after they are dened.
However we also see the syntax is not very nice for total functions, and it is not
so easy, if we want to change this syntax into something simpler. It is easier to
change the syntax accepted for maps.
Another idea is to use object arrays. Using this, one can gather all variables of
eg. Point in the same object. And furthermore, we have an array to collect all
points of a concrete specication. However these object arrays are not supported
in RSL-SAL.
We see that using RSL-SAL, we can specify what we want to. However the
specication will not always be equally pretty, and some extensions to the syntax
==PAGE:51==
3.3 Bit Operators
35
might be needed.
The most elegant solution would probably be to extend the syntax into accepting
arrays.
Furthermore we see that we need to consider how to express the type represent-
ing ids. Here we have chosen to use a subtype of int. Text is not supported by
SAL, so therefore this has not been chosen.
Likewise, we need to specify the type of the entities. We see that there are cases,
where we can use the variant type to specify this. Compared to using subtype
of integer, this can give a more readable solution in many cases. For the most
of the cases in this specic generic model, the solution of using variant types as
described in section 3.2.4.2 will do the trick.
3.3
Bit Operators
For the concrete example, there is used some bit operations. For each direction
in each section, we have a variable HTO, which is used to determine three things:
whether a head of a train is inside the section, whether the tail of a train is inside
the section, and whether the section is occupied by a train. Now let us say, we
move the head of a train from section i to section j, then we have the following
expression
headMove(i) ≡i.HTO&101 = 101 ⇒i.HTO′ = i.HTO⊕100∧j.HTO′ = j.HTO⊕101
If for section i, the head of a train is in the section and the section is occupied,
then we toggle the H-bit for i so that no head is inside the section. Furthermore
we toggle the H-bit and the O-bit for the linear section j, as the head of the
train should move into the linear section j.
These bit operations cannot be specied in RSL at the current moment. One
solution could be for the user specifying the model, to dene a type in RSL
describing bit-strings. Another approach could be to extend the syntax of RSL,
while a third approach could be to rewrite the expression not using bit opera-
tions. A last alternative is to just plainly translate the expression. This way
the user can specify whatever he wants as long as he species according to the
rules of the language he want to translate into.
==PAGE:52==
36
Challenges and Considerations
3.3.1
Specifying Bit String Type
One approach could be to specify a Bitstring type in RSL. If one chose this
approach, the user would need to specify the type in RSL, and then dene the
operations for this type. Here the user would need to specify a function for xor,
and etc.
The BitString type could be specied as the following
type
Bit == on
|
off ,
BitString = Bit >< Bit >< Bit
A BitString is a list of bits, and every bit is either on or o. Now one could
specify a function for the &-operator like the following
and
:
Bit >< Bit −> Bit
and (b1 ,
b2 )
i s
i f
b1 = on /\ b2 = on
then on
e l s e
o f f
end ,
and
:
BitString >< BitString −> BitString
and (( b1a , b1b , b1c ) ,
( b2a , b2b , b2c ))
i s
( and ( b1a , b2a ) , and (b1b , b2b ) , and ( b1c , b2c ))
For this approach we do not need to change the syntax of RSL, and we have
showed a way of dening bit strings in RSL.
However every time the user would use bit strings, he would need to specify the
type himself.
This is just a way for user himself to specify a bit string type. However the
point of using bit operators is performance, and here we just specify bit strings
as a type containing bits.
3.3.2
Extend the Syntax
Another solution would be to extend the syntax so that the bit operators &,
⊕etc are allowed.
This way the user would not need to dene and specify
==PAGE:53==
3.3 Bit Operators
37
the BitString type himself. We could just make these operations allowed for
integers.
3.3.3
Rewrite without Bit Operations
A third solution is to rewrite the expression, so that we do not use bit operators.
Let us look at the formula
headMove(i) ≡i.HTO&101 = 101 ⇒i.HTO′ = i.HTO⊕100∧j.HTO′ = j.HTO⊕101
We see that rst we have i.HTO&101 = 101, and all we use this expression
for, is to check whether the H-bit and the O-bit is set (so we know whether
a head of a train is in the section and whether the section is occupied). This
HTO variable could be split up into three dierent variables, so one variable
describes the head, one describes the tail, and one describes whether the section
is occupied.
Another way to rewrite without using bit operators is to use an invariant type
as described in section 3.2.4.2.
3.3.4
Plain Translation
For this approach we simply plainly translate the expressions specied by the
user. This a simple approach and of course gives the user a lot of freedom. Fur-
thermore according to which language the user want his specication translated
into, he can just specify according to this. However this would not make it
possible for us to type check the user's expression. Furthermore the expression
would be dependent at how he wants the specication translated.
The purpose of specifying such language as this project aims to do, is to make
it possible for the user to do early type-checking in order to prevent errors and
safe time. This is not possible with this solution.
Furthermore the specication here should be independent of the output for
translation, then translators should be specic for the output. This will not be
the case for this approach.
==PAGE:54==
38
Challenges and Considerations
3.3.5
Conclusion
This third solution, rewriting the expressions so that they do not use bit opera-
tions, might be more readable, however eciency is also important for a model.
If one wants to use bit strings it is probably for a reason, as this can improve
performance.
Therefore extending the language into supporting bit operators could be an
useful extension to the language.
3.4
Conclusion
Wee se that using the support of current RSL-SAL, we will be able to specify a
generic model of future danish interlocking systems as we want to do. However
some aspects are very time consuming and error prone to do. Therefore some
extensions to the language such as prioritizing transitions and arrays are very
useful to the language. Furthermore one can imagine other models, where such
constructs desired.
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 4
Denition of Language
Extensions
This chapter describes the extensions that has been implemented to the RSL-
SAL language. The extensions are described by extended grammar rules. The
extension of the type checker is described by inference rules, and the meaning
of the new expressions are illustrated by corresponding SAL code.
4.1
Priorities
4.1.1
Concrete Syntax
We have extended the language to contain the [>]-operator, where it only
supported the [=]-operator before.
As before a transition system consists
of a number of transitions like it also did before, but now we have dierent
operators between the transitions.
We can write up the following grammar
rules as illustrated in gure 4.1.

an operator.
The rule guarded_cmd represents a guarded command, and

this is rule is not changed compared to the original RSL-SAL language. The

We see from the rules that, whenever one starts using one of the operators, eg.

read, eg. (T1[=]T2)[>]T3.
Using these rules, we can write the following
(T1[>]T2)
where T1 and T2 both are a transitional operator.
4.1.2
Static Checks
Type-checking is not dierent from before. Each transition should still all be
type checked individually, and it does not matter for the type checker, whether
transitions are separated by [=] or [>].
4.1.3
Meaning
Looking at the expression
T1[>]T2
This means that the transition T1 have higher priority than T2. Meaning that
transitions from T1 should always be picked before transitions from T2.
Two dierent versions have been implemented in order to handle arrays. The
reason for this is that the rst version is not appropriate for bounded model
checking using induction.
4.1.3.1
Version 1
The expression
. . .
G1 ==> U1 [ >] (G2 ==> U2 [=] G3 ==> U3) [ >] G4 ==> U4
. . .
==PAGE:58==
42
Denition of Language Extensions
where G1, G2, G3 and G4 are some guards of value expressions, U1, U2, U3
and U4 are lists of update expressions, meaning lists of value expressions.
This will be translated into the following in SAL:
. . .
LOCAL
. . .
possible_1_
: BT_AN! Bool_ ,
possible_2_
: BT_AN! Bool_
INITIALIZATION
. . .
possible_1_ = TRUE,
possible_2_ = TRUE
TRANSITION
. . .
G1' ' −−> possible_1_ ' =true ;
possible_2_ ' = true ; U1' '
[ ] NOT G1' ' −−> possible_1_ ' = f a l s e
[ ] NOT possible_1_ AND G2' ' −−> possible_1_ '= true ;
possible_2_ ' = true ; U2' '
[ ] NOT possible_1_ AND G3' ' −−> possible_1_ '= true ;
possible_2_ ' = true ; U3' '
[ ] NOT ( possible_1_ OR (G2' ' OR G3' ' ) ) −−> possible_2_ '=
f a l s e

possible_1_ '= true ;
possible_2_ ' = true ; U4' '
. . .
where G1′′ is the expression G1 translated into SAL, U1′′ is U1 translated into
SAL, and likewise for the other guards and update expressions. possible_1_
and possible_2_ are variables of type boolean instantiated to the true value.
So in other words, if we have transitions dened in the following way T1[>]T2,
where both T1 and T2 represent a set of transitions, then we create an extra
variable called possible_y_, which is initialized to true, and we create an extra
transition. This extra transition checks if the disjunction of all the guards of the
transitions in T1 is false. If this is the case then the value possible_y_ is set
to false. Furthermore, it is appended to all the guards of the transitions in T2
that possible_y_ should be false. This way it is ensured that transitions from
T2 is only chosen if there are no possible transitions in T1. Furthermore it is
appended to all update expressions that all the possible_x_-variables, including
possible_y_, should be set to true, because after we have chosen a transition,
we want to start by trying choosing a transition of high priority.
==PAGE:59==
4.1 Priorities
43
This version is not appropriate for model checking where induction is used.
This is not suited bounded model checking using induction, as we introduce
new variables, meaning the model checker can start from an initial state, where
some possible_y_ is set to false, even though there exists transitions from its
priority level that can be chosen.

Here the DEFINITION keyword is used to ensure that a possible_x_ variable

i

value_expr = . . .
|

[ | [ i

==PAGE:61==
4.2 Arrays
45
4.2.2
Static Checks
If we start by looking at the expression for the type expression for the array
type
ARRAY T1 OF T2
Here we just need to type check for the two type expressions T1 and T2 and
ensure these are correct.
If we have an access to some array by the value expression N[V ], where N is a
value expression describing the array, and V is some value expression describing
the index of the position in the array, we want to access. We check that N is of
type array, and we get the index and value type for this array. We then check
if V is of correct type according to the index type of the array described by N.
An we evaluate the expression to be of type according to the value type of the
array described by N.
Looking at the value expression for specifying an array:
[ | [ T] V| ]
where T is a typing, also containing a type expression and V is a value expres-
sion. Here we do normal type checking on the typing T, which also contain the
index-type of the array. Likewise we do normal type checking on the value ex-
pression V , where we also get the type of that value expression. The expression
is then evaluated to be of array type with index type according to the typing
T, and value type according to the type of V .
Our extension for the update expression, N[I] = V , also needs to be type-
checked.
We do type-checking for the index-value, I, ensuring this has the
correct type and is specied correctly. We check this type according to how N
is dened. Likewise we check that V is also of correct type.
4.2.2.1
Inference Rules
We can write up some inference rules for type checking the arrays.
We write up the inference rules using the following Σ = (T, V L, V R), where T
is a map from type abbreviations into type denitions, V L is a map from value
identiers into type denitions, and V R is a map from variable identiers into
type denitions. T, V L and V R is of the following form:
==PAGE:62==
46
Denition of Language Extensions
T:
Id −m−> Type
VL:
Id −m−> Type
VR:
Id −m−> Type
where Id describe identiers and Type describes type denitions. Possible values
for Type is extended to include a type for arrays:
Type == Int
|
Nat
|
. . .
|
array (Type , Type)
We can now write the following rules:
(T, V L, V R) ⊢N ▷array(T1, T2), rs1, rs2
(T, V L, V R) ⊢I ▷T1, rs2, ws2
(T, V L, V R) ⊢N[I] ▷T2, rs1 ∪rs2, ws1 ∪ws2
This says that N is expression that an be evaluated to be of type array(T1,T2),
and that the type of the expression I should be T1.
This can give us the
expression N[I] of type T2.
Now as we have this, the system already includes other inference rules such as:
(T, V L, V R) ⊢E1 ▷T1, rs1, ws1
(T, V L, V R) ⊢E2 ▷T2, rs2, ws2
op : T1 × T2 →T
(T, V L, V R) ⊢E1 op E2 ▷T, rs1 ∪rs2, ws1 ∪ws2
We see that using these rules, we ca write a statement, such as N[1] = 2, if the
type of N is an array with index type int and value type int, and the = operator
is specied to take in two ints.
The type checker has also been included the following inference rule:
(T, V L ∪[i 7→T1], V R) ⊢E ▷T2, rs, ws
(T, V L, V R) ⊢[|[i : T1]E|] ▷array(T1, T2), rs, ws
This means we can specify an expression describing an array. Using these rules,
one can for instance initialize an array to some value.

Meaning
Let us start by looking at the type expression for array:

where T1 and T2 are type expressions.

in RSL, where N and I are value expressions. This corresponds to the SAL

where N ′′ is the expression N translated to SAL, and I′′ is the expression I
