Chapter 5
Implementaion
This chapter describes how the extensions to the RSL-SAL language has been
implemented. Firstly, it is generally described how the code for RSL-SAL is
organized, and which les have the responsibilities to do the dierent parts of
the tool. Eg. it is described, where the concrete syntax, abstract syntax etc is
dened.
Then it is described, how the priorities extension for transitions is implemented
and lastly how the arrays are implemented. For each of these, it is described,
how the abstract and concrete syntax of RSL is extended, and extensions for
type checker are described. Furthermore, it is described how the SAL-translator
has been extended, including changes of the resolver, the abstract syntax for
representing SAL specications and the translator of abstract RSL syntax into
SAL.
For both priorities and arrays, the pretty-printer (pp.g), and le for printing
error messages (print.g) are also extended, these are just extended in order to
print the new expressions.
==PAGE:66==
50
Implementaion
5.1
General for RSL-SAL
The RSL-SAL tool uses Gentle to type check and translate specications.
The code consist of an ast.g le in which the abstract syntax of a RSL-specication
is dened. Furthermore it consists of a grammar.g le, which reads the concrete
syntax of a RSL-specication and generates a representing of this as an abstract
syntax three according to the abstract syntax three described in ast.g.
The type checking happens in values.g. Here exists some rules for type checking
dierent elements, and also a rule make_results which takes in a VALUE_EXPR
and returns a list of results. Each Result contains a TYPE_EXPR, and also
read-accesses, write-accesses, input-accesses and output-accesses. This way for
a value expression, we can get possible types for the expression, and which
variables it needs access to. The values.g uses for instance types.g when type
checking.
The print.g le includes rules for printing the dierent elements of ast.g. Here
are rules for printing the dierent value expressions, type expression, etc. This
is used for printing error messages. pp.g holds the rules for pretty-printing a
RSL-specication.
The tool also includes a resolver, which resolves the abstract syntax of a RSL-
specication.
This resolver is implemented in the le cc.g.
This resolves a
RSL-specication after it has been type checked and before it is translated into
eg. a SAL-specication.
The source les also include a bunch of les with the prex sal. These are
used by the SAL-translator. The le sal-ast.g denes the abstract syntax three
for a SAL-specication
The biggest part of the translation from RSL to SAL occurs in the le sal_gen_ast.g.
Here especially the rule Gen_SAL_Expr is used to translate a value expression
(VALUE_EXPR) of RSL into a value expression for SAL (SAL_VALUE_EXPR),
where value expressions for SAL are dened in sal_ast.g
In sal_gen_ast.g we create an abstract syntax three of a SAL-specication,
from an abstract syntax three of RSL-specication.
Lastly the sal_gen_code generates SAL-code based on the abstract syntax three
of the SAL-specication.
==PAGE:67==
5.2 Implementation of Priorities
51
5.2
Implementation of Priorities
Two dierent ways of supporting priorities have been implemented, where one
of the versions is not appropriate for bounded model checking using induction.
These two versions are the dierent ways of interpreting such priority as de-
scribed in sections 4.1.3.1 and 4.1.3.2.
5.2.1
General for Both Versions
In this section, implementation details that is common for both versions of
handling prioritized transitions is described.
5.2.1.1
Abstract Syntax of RSL
For implementing the support of prioritizing transitions, we started by looking
at the current RSL code.
Here we saw in the abstract syntax three that a
transition system is a set of guarded commands and a declaration of variables.
We have a type called GUARDED_COMMAND, which can consist of an iden-
tier, a guard being represented as a value expression, and then some commands
describing how the variables should be updated if the transition is chosen. A
GUARDED_COMMAND represents one transition.
Then we had the type GUARDED_COMMANDS, which is just a list of
GUARDED_COMMAND. Then a transition system consists of the declara-
tion of variables and a GUARDED_COMMANDS. In the concrete syntax all
guarded commands are separated by [=] as they all have the same priority.
We then extended the abstract syntax three (ast.g) to contain a type called
TRANSITION_OPERATOR. This can take four dierent values. It can be of
guarded_command, which just contain a GUARDED_COMMAND. It can be
of bracketed, meaning a TRANSITION_OPERATOR contained in parentheses.
It can be of equal_priority, which contains two TRANSITION_OPERATOR,
where the two expressions have the same priority. Lastly it can be of greater_priority
also containing two TRANSITION_OPERATOR, meaning the left-hand side
TRANSITION_OPERATOR has greater priority compared to the right-most.
We have replaced the GUARDED_COMMANDS type with the
TRANSITION_OPERATOR type.
==PAGE:68==
52
Implementaion
5.2.1.2
Concrete Syntax
Then we changed the grammar (grammar.g) so that we can read the symbol [>]
in the concrete syntax, and so that we use the TRANSITION_OPERATOR
type.
Here when we see the symbol [>], we use the greater_priority in-
stance for TRANSITION_OPERATOR, and when we see the [=] we use
the equal_priority instance of TRANSITION_OPERATOR. The grammar.g
le was changed in order to the syntax rules of gure 4.1.
5.2.1.3
Type Checker
The type checker was changed (values.g). This was just changed to use the new
Gentle-type TRANSITION_OPERATOR instead of the type GUARDED_COMMANDS.
Each transition is still type checked the same way as before.
5.2.2
Specic for Version 1
This describes the changes to the SAL translator according to the approach
described in section 4.1.3.1. This version is not appropriate for bounded model
checking using induction, as an initial state, where the possible_ variables are
set to a value that does not truly describe whether there exist a transition in
the specic priority layer that can be chosen.
5.2.2.1
Resolver
In cc.g, we changed the resolver. How an expression should be translated is illus-
trated in section 4.1.3 Firstly we needed to add a number of help variables, called
possible_1_, possible_2_, etc. These were created in the Resolve_trans_sys_def-
rule. This is just done by iterating through the transition system through the
TRANSITION_OPERATORs, and each time a greater_priority element was
met, a new possible_-variable was created. These are all set to be of type Bool
and have the initial value true. We get a possible_-variable for each priority
level, we have in the specication. This is done before resolving the transitions.
The rule for resolving transitions have also been changed. It now takes three
more input, one of them is of type EXTRA_GUARD. This can either be a
value expression or nil. Furthermore it takes an input of type COMMANDS,
which represent the update of the possible_-variables. Each time a transition
==PAGE:69==
5.2 Implementation of Priorities
53
is chosen, all the possible_ variables should be set to true, as we should al-
ways start trying applying the highest prioritized transitions. Lastly it takes in
an integer called Count. The resolver creates extra transitions for setting the
possible_-variables to false if no transitions with the specic priority is possible.
The input-integer Count is used for deciding which possible-variables to update
in the extra transitions.
Below in Figure 5.1 pseudo code for the rule of resolving transitions can be
seen.
The EXTRA_GUARD input is parsed recursively so that each time
we resolve a transition, we know how the guard should be extended.
Ini-
tially the Resolve_transitions rule is called with the EXTRA_GUARD input
set to nil, meaning nothing should be appended to the guards.
Each time,
we reach a greater_priority, eg T1[>]T2, then we know the disjunction of the
guards of T1.
This is in the pseudo-code below represented by EG2.
Fur-
thermore EG is a set of possible_ variables that should all be set to false
in order for the transition to be chosen.
We also have the input variable
Count, describing which possible_-variable should be set in the extra tran-
sition.
Based on this, the extra transition is created.
We extend the EX-
TRA_GUARD we parse down to the right-hand-side transition, with the new
possible_-variable. This way we see that greater_priority(L,R,_) is resolved
into equal_priority(equal_priority(L,ExtraTrans),R), where ExtraTrans is the
extra transition generated, responsible for setting a possible_ variable to false.
The COMMANDS argument is initially value expressions setting all possible_-
variables to true, and this is just recursively parsed through, meaning for all
transitions except the extra transitions, we set all the possible_-variables to
true.
The Resolve_transition action is extended to also take in an EXTRA_GUARD,
which represent a value expression that is disjunction of all the possible_-
variables that should be false for the transition to be chosen. This expression
is negated and conjuncted to the original guard of the transition.
Likewise
Resolve_transition takes in a COMMANDS representing all the possible_-
variables that should be set to true. These commands are just appended to
the original commands of the transition.
5.2.2.2
Translating to SAL
The translator to SAL only had to be changed to use the TRANSITION_OPERATOR.
As the resolver resolves all greater_priority occurrences into equal_priority, the
transitions should still be translated the same way as they did before.
==PAGE:70==
54

==PAGE:71==
5.2 Implementation of Priorities
55
5.2.3
Specic for Version 2
This describes the implementation for the version according to the method de-
scribed in section 4.1.3.2.
Where version 1 as described in section 5.2.2, creates some extra variables, and
some extra transitions that is responsible for setting these variables to true/false
according to whether it is possible to choose a transition from a specic priority
layer, this version also creates extra variables. However this version uses the
DEFINITION keyword of SAL to make sure these variables have the correct
boolean value.
5.2.3.1
Resolver
For the resolver for this version, the abstract syntax of of RSL has been slightly
extended besides the extensions described in section 5.2.1.1.
This has been
extended with two types: DEFINITION, which is just a value expression, rep-
resenting a denition for a possible_x_ variable. And a type DEFINITIONS,
which is a list containing DEFINITION elements.
Likewise the functors for the type RESOLVED_TRANSITION_SYSTEM that
describes a resolved transition system is extended with two elds: PossibleVars
used for describing the possible_x_ variables and Denitions that contains the
denitions of these possible_x_ variables.
The resolver then is responsible for setting these elds, meaning in a resolved
transition system, we know, which possible_x_ variables we need, and how they
should be dened. Furthermore each transition contains an EXTRA_GUARD,
which is value expression or nil. After the transition system has been resolved,
then for each transition, this EXTRA_GUARD is an expression that describes,
which possible_x_ variables should be set to false in order for the transition to
be chosen.
5.2.3.2
Abstract Syntax Describing SAL
Small changes have been applied to the abstract syntax describing the SAL
specications, in the le sal_ast.g.
We have introduced two new types: SAL_DEFINITION, which is a SAL_VALUE_EXPR
(value expression) describing a denition, meaning it should be some assignment
==PAGE:72==
56
Implementaion
of a variable, and SAL_DEFINITIONS which is just a list of SAL_DEFINITION.
The functor module_decl of the type SAL_CONTEXT_CONSTANT, we have
extended to include SAL_DEFINITIONS, representing the denitions of a mod-
ule declaration.
5.2.3.3
Translating to SAL
The SAL translator now takes in a RESOLVED_TRANSITION_SYSTEM,
which now is extended to have variable declarations describing the possible_x_
variables, and denitions of these. The possible_x_ variable declarations are
just concatenated to the local variable declarations, and these are handled the
same way as the local variable declarations. The denitions of the possible_x_
variables are just translated as normal value expressions. The translated de-
nitions is stored in the abstract syntax describing the module which represents
the transition system.
5.2.3.4
Printing SAL Specication
As the abstract syntax for describing a module declaration has been changed in
the sal_ast.g le, the sal_gen_code.g le has been changed in order to print
denitions.
This is just extended to print these as normal value expression,
where the DEFINITION keyword is used.
5.3
Implementation of Arrays
5.3.1
Abstract Syntax of RSL
Firstly the ast.g le was extended so that the abstract syntax of a RSL-specication
can include arrays. Type expressions were included to be able to represent ar-
rays, so the type TYPE_EXPR was extended with a functor
• array(dom_type: TYPE_EXPR, rng_type: TYPE_EXPR).
Now a type expression can represent an array, an such array includes a type
expression representing the domain type (index type) of the array, and a type
==PAGE:73==
5.3 Implementation of Arrays
57
expression representing the range type (value type) of the array.
Furthermore the type VALUE_EXPR is also extended with some functors:
• array_access(pos: POS, name: VALUE_EXPR, index: VALUE_EXPR):
This represents an expression where we access the index of an array. The
eld name is a value expression, meaning this can for instance be the name
of a variable or value, but it can also be any other value expression, but
the value expression must represent an array.
• array_expr(pos: POS, sub: TYPING, val: VALUE_EXPR): This expres-
sion represents an array. The expression include a typing representing the
possible index-values of the array, and a value expression representing the
value at dierent indexes in the array.
• array_assignment(pos:POS, name: NAME, indexes: VALUE_EXPRS,
new_val: VALUE_EXPR): This represents the assignment of a new value
for an index in the array. The index attribute is a list of VALUE_EXPR,
as we might access an array in the 2nd, 3rd etc dimension.
• array_ass_occ(pos: POS, id: Variable_id, qual: OPT_QUALIFICATION,
indexes: VALUE_EXPRS, new_val: VALUE_EXPR): This represents
that a variable, which is an array, represented by an id, is assigned a
new value for a specic index.
The index is represented by a list of
VALUE_EXPR as we might assign a new value to a position in the 2nd,
3rd etc dimension.
Furthermore the type COMMAND was extended. This type represent the up-
dating of a variable in a transition. This type was extended with the functor
• array_cmd(pos: POS, name: NAME, indexes: VALUE_EXPRS, up-
date_expr: VALUE_EXPR): This represents the update of an array at a
specic index-position, and again the index is a list of VALUE_EXPR as
we might update the value of an array in the 2nd or 3rd etc dimension.
5.3.2
Concrete Syntax
The grammar.g le has been extended so that it accepts the concrete syn-
tax for the arrays, as it is described in section 4.2.
This way the nonterm
sal_update_expr, which represent the reading of an update expression in a
==PAGE:74==
58
Implementaion
transition, is extended to include a rule for reading an expression, where we as-
sign a new value to a specic index of an array. The update expession is stored
using the array_cmd functor, as we want to store the name of the variable, and
we want to know at what index position (perhaps in a dimension greater than
1) we will update, and we need to know the new value. Likewise a rule has
been included in order to accept type expressions of arrays, and lastly the le
was extended so a value expression could be read as an array_access and as an
array_expr.
The grammar.g le never creates value expressions using the functors array_assignment
and array_ass_occ, these are used for resolving and translating into SAL.
5.3.3
Type Checker
The le values.g was extended to type check arrays. It was extended to type
check the new kind of update expressions, meaning of type COMMAND using
the functor array_cmd. Likewise this le has been extended to type check value
expressions of array_access and array_expr according to the rules described in
section 4.2.2.
When we need to type check a value expression described by array_access(pos,name,index),
we check that name is of array type, and we check that the type of index corre-
sponds to the index type of this array.
For the array_cmd(pos, name, indexes, update_expr), we check that the vari-
able described by name is of type array. Recursively we check the expressions
in the list indexes and the type of name. This recursive function takes in the
type of name and the list indexes, each time it checks that the type is an array,
and that the index type of the array is compatible with the type of the rst
expression in indexes. This function calls itself recursively with the range type
of the array as input for type, and where the currently rst element of the list
indexes is removed. If the list indexes only contains one element, then the range
type of the array type is returned. Lastly it is checked if the update_expr is
compatible with this returned type. This way this function checks that all the
index expressions have the correct type according to the type of name. And we
check that the expression accessing the array is of same type as update_expr.
==PAGE:75==
5.3 Implementation of Arrays
59
5.3.4
Abstract Syntax Describing SAL
The abstract syntax for a SAL-specication has been extended in the sal_ast.g
le. The type MACRO_CONSTANT has been extended to include functors
macro_array_cmd and macro_cc_array_cmd. These are used to generate the
prelude les for array types. These prelude les include operations for arrays.
Two functions exists, access(i,a), for accessing array a at index i, and set(i,v,a)
for setting the value of array a at index i to v. There is generated a le for each
array type in the specication, as these functions are dependent on the domain
and range type of the arrays.
Furthermore the type SAL_VALUE_EXPR has been extended with the fol-
lowing functors:
• sal_array_expr(sub: SAL_BINDINGS, type: SAL_TYPE_EXPR, val:
SAL_VALUE_EXPR): This represents an array for SAL of type. sub
represents the index-values, and val represents the value for the dierent
indexes.
• sal_array_access(name: SAL_VALUE_EXPR, index: SAL_VALUE_EXPR):
This represents the access of an array at some index. name is a value
expression representing the array, while index is a value expression repre-
senting the index position.
• sal_cc_array_access(name: SAL: SAL_VALUE_EXPR,
index: SAL_VALUE_EXPR): This represents the access of an array for
the cc-version.
• sal_array_var_occ(Pos: POS, VarId: SAL_variable_id,
Indexes: SAL_VALUE_EXPRS): This represents an occurrence of a vari-
able of array-type, where we access the array at position described by the
value expressions of Indexes. Indexes is a list of value expressions as we
might access an array at the position in a dimension greater than one, eg.
a[0][0].
• sal_cc_array_set(Pos: POS, Array: SAL_VALUE_EXPR,
Type: SAL_TYPE_EXPR, Indexes: SAL_VALUE_EXPRS,
Value: SAL_VALUE_EXPR). This represents an expression in the cc-
version, where we want to set the value of an array at the index represented
by the expressions in the list Indexes. Again Indexes is a list of value
expression, as we might set the value of an array with dimension greater
than one. Array describes the array, we want to update. This is either a
variable occurrence, or of functor sal_cc_array_set. Type describes the
type of Array.
==PAGE:76==
60
Implementaion
5.3.5
Translating to SAL
The sal_gen_ast le has been extended in order to translate the array ex-
pressions. The SAL_Gen_Type_Expr action has been extended to be able to
generate array types.
The Gen_SAL_Expr has been extended to take in functors array_expr and ar-
ray_access for VALUE_EXPR, and this le generate SAL_VALUE_EXPR ac-
cording to this. This can generate SAL_VALUE_EXPR of functors sal_array_expr,
sal_array_access and sal_cc_array_access.
Lastly Gen_SAL_Cmds has been updated, so this checks whether it is a normal
assign occurrence or an array assignment occurrence. If it is an array assignment,
we generate SAL_VALUE_EXPR using the functors sal_array_var_occ,
sal_cc_array_var_occ and sal_cc_array_set.
The sal_aux.g le was also extended to handle array type. This le includes
actions for handling the map and set types, and this has been extended so that
it can also handle array type. This is for handling the types of arrays in the
cc-version.
5.3.5.1
CC-version
In the cc-version, we use a lifted type for representing arrays. This lifted type
can either be a nav value or an array. Therefore for updating the value of an
array at some index, we need to assign new value to the variable describing the
array. This means an expression like:
a ' [ i ] = v
is translated into SAL
a ' = array_set ( i ' ' , v ' ' , a )
Here i is a value expression specifying the index, we look at, and v is a value
expression specifying the new value at this index.
i′′ is an SAL expression
representing i and v′′ is a SAL expression representing v.
This function array_set is generated by the prelude le for arrays, and it takes in
an index-value, a value representing the new value for the array at the specied
index, and then an array. This functions returns an array, where all indexes
have the same value as for the array a except for the position at index i, this
==PAGE:77==
5.3 Implementation of Arrays
61
value is set to v. This way we assign a new value to the position at i for the
array a.
If we have a transition, where we are setting the value for dierent indexes of
the array like this:
a ' [ i1 ] = v1
a ' [ i2 ] = v2
we translate into:
a ' = array_set ( i1 ' ' , v1 ' ' , array_set ( i2 ' ' , v2 ' ' , a ))
We are not allowed to assign a new value to the same variable twice in the same
transition, and this way we assure that the value is updated both at index i1
and i2.
Likewise, as this is a condence condition checker, we need to check the type of
the expressions, meaning i′′ is of the form:
i f
is_t1 ( i ' ' ' )
then i ' ' '
e l s e
nav
where is_t1 is a function that checks if i′′′ is of the type t1, and where t1 is the
index type of the array represented by a. Likewise there is a check on v1, v2
and i2. This way if we have some illegal expression in this, the array a will be
set to have a nav-value, and during checking of the cc-version, the user will be
told, the specication is not legal.
Likewise if we have an expression in our RSL specication, where we access an ar-
ray, the sal_gen_ast.g le will generate an expression using the sal_cc_array_access
functor for the cc-version. Then the access-method of arrays is used to access
the array. Likewise here is generated a check that the expression describing
the index is in the type describing the domain type of the array, otherwise a
nav-value is generated.
5.3.5.2
Further Implementations
Furthermore rules has been added to actions SAL_compute_xed_point in le
sal_type_xed_point.g and SAL_Modify_Expr in le sal_gen_code.g.
The SAL_compute_xed_point is responsible for eg that denitions of index
and range types of maps are printed before the denition of the map is printed.
==PAGE:78==
62
Implementaion
This le has been extended to ensure that denitions of index and value types
of arrays are printed before the denition of the array.
The SAL_Modify_Expr action is used for instance when we have an expression
like the following:
e x i s t s !
e
: T :−P( e )
Such expression is not supported by SAL, and the SAL_Modify_Expr is for
instance used in connection to convert the above expression into something
looking like the following:
EXISTS ( e
: T) : P( e ) AND (FORALL (e_ : T) : P(e_) => e_ = e )
Therefore the SAL_Modify_Expr action is modied to also convert array ex-
pressions.
5.3.6
Printing SAL Specication
The sal_gen_code.g is changed so that it can print the extended functors of
the abstract syntax three for SAL.

Chapter 6
Bug Fixes
In this chapter, we have identied some problems with the current RSL-SAL
tool and translator into SAL. These problems are described and illustrated by
an example specication. Furthermore for each problem, a solution is given and
how it has been xed.
6.1
Problem 1
6.1.1
Description
When trying to access a map value at a position which is not in the domain type
of the map, we get runtime error for the cc-version. Instead we would expect
the run to terminate with an invalid assertion. This problem applies only to the
cc-version.
6.1.2
Example Specication
An example specication illustrating the problem can be seen in Figure 6.1
==PAGE:80==
64
Bug Fixes
scheme Example1 =
c l a s s
type
Subint = {| i
:
Int :−i
i s i n
{0..2}
|}
transition_system
[ System ]
l o c a l
m :
Subint −m−> Subint := [0 +> 0 , 1 +> 1 , 2 +> 2] ,
v :
Subint := 0
in
true ==> v ' = m(4)
end
end
Figure 6.1: Example specication illustrating problem 1
This can be type checked and translated successfully into SAL, as we would
expect, and the well-formed checker for SAL is also successful for both the non-
cc-version and the cc-version. However when trying to run the model checker
for the cc-version, we would expect it to terminate with an assertion checking
condence conditions to be invalid, as we are trying to access index 4 in the map
m, and since 4 is not in the domain of m. However instead the model checker
stops with a runtime error.
6.1.3
Solution
The problem for this is how the operation m(4) is translated. This is for the
original RSL-SAL translated for SAL into:
Bool__cc_OPS ! is_true (Subint_Bool__map_cc_OPS ! apply (
L_BUILTIN! Int__cc (4) , m) )
It try to give 4 as a argument for the apply-function, and then when running,
we get an error saying that 4 is not a possible value in this function. Instead it
should translate into the following:
Bool__cc_OPS ! is_true (Subint_Bool__map_cc_OPS ! apply ( IF
Bool__cc_OPS ! is_true (SAL_TYPES_cc! RSL_is_Subint (
L_BUILTIN! Int__cc (4) ) )
THEN L_BUILTIN! Int__cc (4)
==PAGE:81==
6.1 Problem 1
65
ELSE L_BUILTIN! Int__nav(L_BUILTIN!
Argument_being_inserted_in_collection_out_of_subtype )
ENDIF, m) .
For the argument 4 for the apply-function, we rst check if the value is in the
domain type of the map. If it is, then we just pass 4 to the function. Otherwise
we pass a nav-value to the function, meaning the apply function will return
a nav-value, which in the above specication Example1, would mean that i is
assigned a nav-value, which breaks the cc-check-assertion.
6.1.3.1
Fixes in Code
This changes have been implemented to RSL-SAL. Generally when translating
the arguments to applications, now we have the subtype condition check for all
arguments in the application. When we have an argument arg to an application,
we now translate into the following expression
i f
is_subtype ( arg ) then arg
e l s e
nav
If the argument is in the subtype, we pass the argument, otherwise we pass a
nav-value.
6.1.4
Conclusion
The translator has been changed, so that when trying to access an illegal element
of a map, then it is evaluated to a nav-value instead of giving a runtime error.
This example will have an invalid assertion telling something is wrong instead
of throwing a runtime error.
For the above specication, the model checker
will terminate correctly for the translated cc-version having an invalid assertion
telling us, we have an illegal expression, instead of throwing a runtime exception.

6.2
Problem 2
6.2.1
Description
The x of problem 1 leads to another problem. The translated cc-version does
not do any checks on whether the guards contain any illegal expressions. The
translated version for the original code contains an assertion that checks that
no variables are ever given a nav-value (meaning it has been given an illegal
value according to the scope of the variables type). However it does not check
whether the guards contain any illegal expressions. This problem applies only
to the cc-version.
6.2.2
Example Specication
In Figure 6.2 one can see an example specication illustrating this problem.
Running the original translator of RSL to SAL for this example will result in a
specication in SAL that passes the wellformedness check, but gives a runtime
error like for the problem described in section 6.1. However if one introduces
the x described in section 6.1, then the running the SAL model checker for
the cc-version will terminate successfully not nding any problems with valid
assertions. This is not as we would expect as we have an illegal expression in
==PAGE:83==
6.2 Problem 2
67
the guard, m(4). In the guard of the transition, we try to access m(4) even
though 4 is not in the domain of the subtype describing the map's domain-type.
Introducing the x of Problem 1, then m(4) will evaluate to nav-value in the
cc-version, and this is just handled by returning false for the guards.
6.2.3
Solution
The solution to this have been to introduce an assertion for the cc-version that
checks that the guards does not contain a nav-value, as well as we already have
an assertion checking that no variables has a nav-value.
If in a guard, we have an illegal expression, the expression will be evaluated to
nav-value, and then as this value is used to other operations, the result of these
operations will also be a nav-value. Meaning if we have an illegal expression
somewhere in the guard then the guard will evaluate to a nav-value.
6.2.3.1
Fixes in Code
The assertion checking whether the guards contain nav-values have been imple-
mented in the same manor as the assertion checking the variables for nav-values.
For each guard, it is checked that the guard is a boolean value, and then this is
concatenated into one expression checking that all guards are always evaluated
to a boolean value.
If for instance, we have the two guards a and b, then the assertion would look
like the following:
is_bool ( a ) AND is_bool (b)
where is_bool checks that the expression is evaluated to a boolean.
If for
instance a is in a comprehended expression, where i iterates through some type
t, then the assertion would look like:
(FORALL( i
:
t )
:
is_bool ( a )) AND is_bool (b)
6.2.4
Conclusion
Now we have an assertion checking that guards does not contain nav-values.
Before we could not be certain that the cc-version would nd an error in our
==PAGE:84==
68
Bug Fixes
specication of guards. However now, they will.
6.3
Problem 3
6.3.1
Description
For the cc-version, variables are given a lifted type. This lifted type can either
be a nav-value (describing condence condition is broken) or it can describe the
value of the variable. This problem lies within comprehended transitions. If
we have a comprehended transition running through all elements in a subtype,
then in the cc-version, this is translated into running through all elements in
the maximal type. This is illustrated in the example below.
This problem means, that even though a specication is specied correctly,
running the cc-version, will tell you that you have broken some condence con-
ditions.
This problem applies only to the cc-version.
6.3.2
Example Specication
An example specication can be seen in Figure 6.3.
In the transition, we want to run through all elements in the type Subint, which
is a subtype of Int.
For the specic example, we want i to iterate through
{0,1,2}, as these are in the domain of the map m. However when translated,
the cc-version will set i to iterate through all integers (within the scope of the
global minimum and global maximum).
For this example, even though it is specied correctly, we get a runtime error
when running the cc-version.
6.3.3
Solution
For the example illustrated here, the transition would be translated into
[ ]
( i : IT_AN! Int_ ) :

Fixes in Code
This has been implemented. When we have a comprehended transition, then
instead iterating through the type representing the maximal type, we just use
the type, that was there originally.
6.3.4
Conclusion
When having comprehended expressions, in the cc-version, we only iterate through
elements of the type, we do not use the maximal type anymore. This means that
before the cc-version could have invalid assertions even though the specication
was okay due to comprehended expressions, now this does not happen anymore.

INITIALIZATION
a = Subint2_set_OPS ! add (0 , Subint2_set_OPS ! empty_set ) ;
b = Subint2_set_OPS ! add (0 , Subint2_set_OPS ! empty_set ) ;
Some operations have been introduced in order to generate and use sets. We see
that a is given the correct type, but we see that operations according to generate
variables of the type Subint2-set is used to set the value of a. This means when
trying to run the wellformedness checker of the translated SAL-code, we get an
==PAGE:88==
72
Bug Fixes
error of incompatible types. The operation Subint2_set_OPS!add returns of
type Subint2_set.
6.4.3
Solution
The solution to this problem is to ensure, when a has the type Subint_set, then
the operations assigned to this type should be used instead of the operations
assigned to Subint2_set.
6.4.3.1
Fixes in Code
When the translator generates a new set type, we assign some operations to the
set type. These are represented by an identier attached to the type. Somehow
both set types Subint_set and Subint2_set are assigned the same identier,
and since the Subint2_set is dened lastly, the operations of this is used. It has
been xed so that the two set types use dierent operations, and that they use
the correct denitions.
6.4.4
Conclusion
This has been xed so that a user can have dierent set types, so that user can
both have a set of Subint and Subint2.
Furthermore for the cc-version, an element of Subint-set was translated in an el-
ement of Int-set. This is also changed, so that in the cc-version, it is translated
into a lifted type that can either be nav or a Subint_set.
Before translat-
ing the example above to SAL, the elements a and b would be given a type
SAL_TYPES_cc!Int__set_cc, which can either be nav or a set of ints.
However as a is a set of Subint, then there is no reason for the variable to be
able to be a set of ints. Therefore this variable should in the cc-version either
be a nav or a set of Subint. And therefore this is changed, so that a in the
cc-version now is given a type SAL_TYPES_cc!Subint__set_cc.
In the original tool, we had problems when trying to dene a set of a subtype.
In the original tool, when dening a set type, one should use a maximal type,
but this has been xed, so now one can have sets of subtypes.

6.5
Problem 5
6.5.1
Description
When we have some record type that can both contain some complex elds,
eg sets, and simple elds, eg Int, then we have a problem in the translation of
the arguments of the mk-function. This means that the wellformedness checker
of the cc-version fails for the translated code. This problem applies to the cc-
version.
6.5.2
Example Specication
An example specication illustrating the problem is shown in Figure 6.5.
For this example, the translator terminates without errors, but the wellformedness-
checker reveals errors in the SAL-code.

We see that the function checks for nav-values and that the arguments to the

Somehow the

This was xed to ensure that the variable was treated like a local variable, so
that the correct variables was passed.

Arguments to mk-functions are now always treated as local variables, so that
the lookup does not fail.
6.5.4
Conclusion
This problem has been xed, so that the translator translate correctly. Trans-
lating the above example using extended RSL-SAL results in a specication in
SAL that is wellformed, and that terminates correctly.
6.6
Problem 6
6.6.1
Description
If the specication includes a nesting of comprehended transitions the original
translator cannot complete. In this case the type checker completes successfully,
but the translator cannot complete.
6.6.2
Example Specication
An example specication illustrating this problem can be seen in Figure 6.6.
Here we see that we have a comprehended transition that includes a compre-
hended transition. When trying to translate this, the translator stop unexpect-
edly with an error message that at some point no rule was applicable.
The way the translator works is that it takes an abstract syntax three as input,
and tries to apply some actions and rules to this in order translate into an
abstract syntax representing the SAL code, which is then used to print the
SAL-specication.
==PAGE:92==

Bug Fixes

([=]

Figure 6.6: Example specication of problem 6
6.6.3
Solution
The translator includes an action (SAL_Process_Transition in sal_gen_ast.g)
for processing transitions, and here the problem was that every time it took in
a comprehended transition, then it assumed that this comprehended transition
included a normal transition and never another comprehended transition, even
though this was accepted by the type checker.
6.6.3.1
Fixes in Code
The actions processing transitions (SAL_Process_Transition in sal_gen_ast.g)
was adjusted so that it took into account that comprehended transitions might
include another comprehended transition.
6.6.4
Conclusion
The x has been implemented so that we can have any number of nested com-
prehended transitions. Using the updated tool, trying to run the example above,
it translates successfully and correctly into SAL.

following way as described in 6.7.

6.7
Problem 7
6.7.1
Description
This problem is that the translator has an error, when translating a specica-
tion, where one has a type that is subtype of another subtype. For this case,
the tool will create cc-version for the SAL-specication that will not pass the
wellformedness checker, even though the RSL-specication is wellformed. This
applies only to the cc-version.
6.7.2
Example Specication
An example describing the specic problem is described in Figure 6.8.
The RSL type checker will succeed successfully, and so will the translation into
SAL. However when running the sal-wfc command of the translated SAL code,
the user will get an error for the cc-version. The translator generates for this
==PAGE:94==
78
Bug Fixes
scheme Example7 =
c l a s s
type
Subint1 = {| i
:
Int :−i
i s i n
{0 ,1 ,2}|} ,
Subint2 = {| i
:
Subint1 :−i
i s i n
{0 ,1}|}
transition_system
[ System ]
l o c a l
a
:
Subint2 := 1
in
a > 0 ==> a ' = 0
end
end
Figure 6.8: An example specication describing the problem 7.
two functions RSL_is_Subint1 and RSL_is_Subint2. These both take an in-
teger as input and returns true if the integer is in the subtype of respectively
Subint1 and Subint2. As Subint2 is a subtype of Subint1, the RSL_is_Subint2
function references the RSL_is_Subint1 function. However the problem is that
in the translated le, then RSL_is_Subint2 is dened before RSL_is_Subint1,
meaning that at the point RSL_is_Subint2 references RSL_is_Subint1, then
RSL_is_Subint1 is not dened yet:
RSL_is_Subint2 ( i : L_BUILTIN! Int__cc ) : L_BUILTIN! Bool__cc
=
IF Bool__cc_ops ! cc_and ( RSL_is_Subint ( i ) ,
. . . )
. . .
ENDIF;
RSL_is_Subint1 ( i : L_BUILTIN! Int__cc ) : L_BUILTIN! Bool__cc
= . . .
6.7.3
Solution
When the translator creates a RSL_is_-function (used for checking if a value
is in a specic subtype), the translator inserts the newly created function into a
list of such functions. The problem is that the translator inserts the new element
in the front of the list. As the translator makes sure that if the denition of
Subint2 is dependent of the type Subint1, then the Subint1 type will be handled
before Subint2 is handled by the translator, meaning that the RSL_is_Subint1
==PAGE:95==
6.8 Problem 8
79
function is created before the RSL_is_Subint2 function. Therefore when the
translator creates new RSL_is_-functions, it should always insert the new func-
tion at the end of the list. The translator has been changed for this.
6.7.3.1
Fixes in Code
In the sal_cc_aux.g le, we have an action (Collect_Is_Type_Function) for
collecting the is_type functions. Every time, a new RSL_is_-function is cre-
ated, this rule is used to insert the new function in a list representing such
functions. This rule is changed to insert elements in the end of the list instead
of front of the list.
6.7.4
Conclusion
This problem has been xed, so that now the cc-version also supports having
types that is subtype of another subtype.
For the above example, the well-
formedness checker for SAL will fail for the cc-version translated SAL speci-
cation when using the original RSL-SAL tool. Using the extended RSL-SAL
tool will generate a SAL specication, where the wellformedness-checker of the
cc-version terminates successfully, saying the specication is wellformed as we
would expect. The SAL model checker can be used to check the cc-version of
the generated SAL specications.
This x does not inuence the non-cc-version, where the original RSL-SAL tool
behaves as we would expect.
6.8
Problem 8
6.8.1
Description
In a RSL-specication, we would expect the type checker to discover, if a guard
is not of type boolean, however if the RSL specication of a transition system
contains a guard that is of a type that is dierent from boolean, this is not
discovered until the translated SAL specication is checked for wellformedness.
==PAGE:96==
80
Bug Fixes
scheme Example8 =
c l a s s
transition_system
[ System ]
l o c a l
a
:
Int := 1
in
3 ==> a ' = 0
end
end
Figure 6.9: Example specication for Problem 8.
6.8.2
Example Specication
An example specication illustrating this problem is shown in Figure 6.9.
Here we would expect the type checker to give an error as the guard in the
transition system evaluates to an integer, where it should evaluate to a boolean.
For the original RSL-SAL, the type checker terminates successfully, and likewise
does the translator into SAL specication. However when the translated SAL
specications are checked for wellformedness according to the sal-wfc command,
then the user is told there is a type mismatch. It sees an integer, where it expects
a boolean.
6.8.3
Solution
The solution to this is to include that guard expressions should be type checked
according to being boolean. Originally RSL-SAL type checks the guard expres-
sion that there is no type mismatching inside the guards. However it is not
checked what type the whole guard expression represents. It is included in the
type checker that it should check if a guard is of type boolean.
6.8.3.1
Fixes in Code
In cc.g for the rule of type checking a single transition, it is included that the
guard expression must be of type boolean.
==PAGE:97==
6.8 Problem 8
81
6.8.4
Conclusion
The tool has been updated so that now if a specication of a transition system
in RSL-SAL, includes a guard expression that is not evaluated to be of type
boolean, then the type checker fails. For the example specication, the updated
tool will fail, when the specication is type checker.

Chapter 7

This chapter describes an example specication of a harbour, where for instance

model checker for the example can be seen in chapter 9
7.1
Specication
An example specication have been implemented in RSL-SAL. This example is
an harbour having some berths and ships. Then ships can enter and leave the
harbour. The specication can be seen in appendix B.
We see that for each index representing a berth, we want a value of type oc-

occupied. Here for the example, we have used an array, so this small specica-
tion gives an example of how arrays can be used.

Chapter 8
Railway Example
This chapter gives a specication of a generic model for future danish inter-
locking systems. The specication is described in this chapter, and furthermore
the specication is discussed. For seeing experimental results of checking this
generic model for dierent instantiations, see chapter 9.
8.1
Specication
A generic model has been specied for interlocking systems[VHP14].
This
generic model can be seen in appendix C. An overview of the les of the generic
model can be seen in gure 8.1.
T
This includes a denition of the basic types of the model. It includes def-
inition of ids of sections, routes and signals, and likewise types for describing
direction and the position of points. The types describing the ids are sets of
integers, which depends on the specic example. Therefore this le has to be
generated for a specic example.
==PAGE:102==
86
Railway Example
Figure 8.1: An overview of the les of the generic model.
==PAGE:103==
8.1 Specication
87
Network
This includes denition of types for describing elements of the net-
work, such as linear sections, points and marker boards. The denition of these
types uses the types dened in T.rsl.
Interlocking
This is for describing the routes, route table and interlocking
system.
IxlTableGenerator
This is used for collecting the Interlocking and Network
les. This is used for generating routetable, network and interlocking according
to the denitions of these type in Network.rsl and Interlocking.rsl.
Generated
This denes an interlocking system, which it does by dening a
network and a route table. This le denes a specic network and a specic route
table, and therefore this le is dependent on the specic example. This uses the
types of Network.rsl and Interlocking.rsl to dene an interlocking system.
Static
This includes a set of functions, which are used to get information
about elements of the network and routetable.
Occupancy
This denes the type used for describing occupancy of a section.
Likewise it includes functions for when trains are entering/leaving sections, and
functions for checking the occupancy of sections. If one desires to modify the
model so that for instance bit operators are used to move trains, this can be
done by modifying this le.
Model
This le includes the transition system for the model. These tran-
sitions uses the functions of Static.rsl to extract static information about the
network. Likewise it uses the functions of Occupancy.rsl to illustrate movement
of trains. The dynamic information of the railway system is stored in variables
in this le.
This le also includes a set of assertions that can check safety
properties of the specication.
==PAGE:104==
88
Railway Example
8.1.1
Generation of T.rsl and Generated.rsl
We see that the les T.rsl and Generated.rsl here has some value denitions that
are not assigned a value. In order to translate into SAL, one needs to assign
a value to these. For T.rsl this is the values pointIds, routeIds, linearIds and
sigIds, and for Generated.rsl, it is the values all_linears, all_points, all_sigs,
all_lcs and all_routes plus a number of auxiliary values.
For the purpose of generating these values a small program has been written that
takes in a xml-conguration of an interlocking system and generates three les,
where two of them are T.rsl and Generated.rsl. These two les can be used to
translate an instantiation of the generic model into SAL and test the assertions.
The third le generated is a le that can be used to check wellformedness of the
specication. This is done according the specication of phd-student Linh Hong
Vu, which can be found at http://www2.compute.dtu.dk/ lvho/rsl/ixldsl.tar.gz[Vu].
For the railway system, the model have been tried on 5 dierent examples called
toy, typical, cross, gt-hd and kj. The xml les can be seen in appendix F. Files
illustrating toy, typical and cross graphically can be seen E. The kj example cor-
responds to the danish train station Køge. The output les T.rsl, Generated.rsl
and GeneratedForWFCheck.rsl for the toy example can be seen in appendix D.
We see that, we have a structure of les that can describe interlocking systems,
also containing functions for extracting information of elements in the interlock-
ing system. However the specication of the generic model is only partly based
on these les. The reason for this is that these les include many elements of
list type and also recursive functions. These two things are not supported by
the RSL-SAL to SAL translator.
8.1.2
Auxiliary Value Denitions
Looking at the Generated.rsl le for the various examples, for instance as in
appendix D.1.2, we see that we generate a bunch of auxiliary values, for instance
we create a value that represent the set of linear sections that has a neighbour
in the down direction.
Logically this is not needed, as one could check the
neighboursLin eld for the linear sections to see if they have neighbours in the
down direction.
We do this because SAL has a problem with lazily evaluating expressions, when
we have a record type containing a map, an we try accessing this map. This is
discussed further in section 8.3.1.
==PAGE:105==
8.2 Global Model Checker vs Bounded Model Checker
89
8.2
Global Model Checker vs Bounded Model Checker
For the global model checker, the specication described here is enough. For
the global model checker, we have initially assigned a value to all the variables.
But for the bounded model checker using induction, we need to specify some
strengthening properties, since we must make sure that the initial state is a valid
state. For instance two conicting routes cannot both be locked in the initial
state and so on. For this the Model.rsl le has been extended, where especially
extra assertions have been added. Some of these extra assertions introduce the
problem with arrays illustrated in section 8.3.1. Therefore for simplicity, the
index type of the arrays are set to be SecId instead of using LinearSectionId
and PointId. This is in particularly for the arrays, PointPosVar, PointCmdVar,
DownToUp, UpToDown, StemToPM, PlusToStem and MinusToStem.
Furthermore as in SAL the bounded model checker takes in an assertion as input
parameter, all the assertions for this are conjuncted into one assertion, so this
conjuncted assertion can be parsed as argument. To see the Model.rsl le that
is specic for the version including the strengthened invariants, see appendix
C.9.
We therefore also have specied the model, where strengthened assertions are
included.
8.3
Discussion
8.3.1
Auxiliary Value Denitions
We see that the automated generated les contains a large number of auxiliary
value denitions, which is not the most pretty solution. However this is necessary
for the model to complete in SAL. A small example illustrating this problem
can be seen in Figure 8.2.
This small specication can successfully be translate into SAL, however when
executing this, we get an error that the array does not have a nite domain or
the argument is not in the domain of the array. The SAL team has been asked
about this behavior, and their response can be seen in appendix G. Apparently
the behavior is a result of the denition of the type Subint2. SAL is not able
to check whether this has a nite type, as in the translated SAL specication
Subint2 is dened as:

==PAGE:107==
8.3 Discussion
91
Subint2 : TYPE = { i : IT_AN! Int_
| Int__set_OPS ! i s i n ( i ,
Int__set_OPS ! add (1 , Int__set_OPS ! empty_set ) ) };
The Subint2 type is dened as integers that satisfy a predicate. SAL does not
know that only the integer 1 satises the predicate and SAL does not know that
the domain type of ar1 is nite. Therefore, one can change the denition of
Subint2 in the SAL specication to following:
Subint2 : TYPE = [1
. .
1 ] ;
This corresponds to if one changes the denition of Subint2 in the RSL speci-
cation into:
Subint2 = {| i
:
Int :−i
i s i n
{ 1 . . 1 } | } ,
Now SAL know this is nite type. However this just leads to a new error when
running the SAL specication. Now it complains on the lines of the initialization
of the rec2 and rec3. Here it complains that 2 and 3 are not in the type of
Subint2, even though the type of the eld var1 of Rec is of type Subint1 and
not Subint2. It complains, because getVar returns this eld, var1, of a Rec, and
since we in our transitions, set i2 to be the returned value of getVar, then i2 can
be any value in the set {1,2,3}, and apparently even though, we have a check
that i2 is equal to 1, then SAL still complains.
This behavior happens, when we are accessing elds of the record types. There-
fore all these auxiliary value denitions are introduced to eliminate these error
messages.
The auxiliary value denitions are used by various functions in the Static.rsl
le. If one wanted to eliminate these auxiliary value denitions, one can redene
these functions of Static.rsl so that the auxiliary value denitions are not used
anymore.
Instead of auxiliary value denitions, an alternative could be to extend the
domain type of the arrays. The problem happens for our Occupancy arrays
(UpToDown, StemToPM etc), and these arrays either have LinearSectionId or
PointId as the domain types. In our transition system, if we for instance want
to check for a section that it is free, then we need to check whether the section
is a point of a linear section, so we know which array to access. And this gives
the problem in SAL, as SAL only know the index-value we try to access is a
SecId (union of PointId and LinearSectionId). Therefore instead of introducing
auxiliary value denitions, one could specify the domain of the 7 arrays (Point-
PosVar, PointCmdVar, UpToDown, DownToUp, StemToPM, MinusToStem and
==PAGE:108==
92
Railway Example
PlusToStem) to have domain type SecId instead. However this would also give
an overhead as it does not make sense to have for instance UpToDown for a
point.
Another alternative to avoid this could be to try and avoid the use of Record
type in the specication.
8.3.2
Level Crossings and Distances
For checking the model, we do not use the level crossings or the length of the
sections. For the model, we do not work with the sizes of the trains. Trains
are modeled by variables on the sections. These variables describe whether the
sections are occupied, contain the head of a train and whether they contain
the tail of a train, and then for the train movement, we move the heads and
the tails of trains from section to section. This means that it is possible for a
train that rst the head moves a couple of sections forward and then the tail
comes afterwards, meaning for the model the trains do not have an absolute
length. This means that we do not use the length of the sections for checking
the safety properties. Likewise the level crossings are not used for checking the
safety properties. Therefore when generating a RSL-specication of an example
from the XML-specication, we do not store the distances and level crossings,
and they are left out from the RSL specication.
The program for generating RSL specication of the example also generates a le
that can be used for checking the wellformedness of the specication according to
the specications of http://www2.compute.dtu.dk/ lvho/rsl/ixldsl.tar.gz. For
this le the level crossings is included.
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 9

This chapter describes the experimental results of running the SAL model
checker on the translated specication instantiations toy, cross, typical gt-hd
and kj for the generic model for interlocking systems described in chapter 8 and
also for the Harbour specication described in chapter 7.
For running tests with SAL, version 3.3 of SAL has been used.
These tests have been performed on both the gbar for DTU, and on another
server which also has been used for getting experimental results for comparing
with RT-Tester tool.
The architecture specications for the gbar at DTU for running the tests is on
CPU model AMD Opteron(tm) Processor 6376 and further specications can
be seen in gure 9.1.
The server specications for the other server used for comparing results with
the RT-Tester tool is Intel(R) Xeon(R) CPU E5-2667 0 @ 2.90GHz, where the
server has 24 cores, and the total memory is 65943344 kB (around 64GB).
The test results of using the RT-Tester tool, we compare with, is run on Intel(R)
Core(TM) i7-3520M CPU @ 2.90GHz, 8GB RAM, Ubuntu 14.04 LTS, Linux
3.14.1-031401-generic x86_64 kernel.

9.2.3
Compare of Priority Versions
Tests on the gbar of DTU has been performed in order to see how the two
dierent versions of implementing the priorities of transitions perform.
The results of these tests can be seen in table 9.4. Here, we have run, where
the system is translated in the most naive way, without introducing helping
variables as also described in section 3.1.1.1. This is in the table 9.4 referred
to as the naive version, as this is the most naive way to implement priorities
of transitions. In the table 9.4, 1st version refers to the approach described in
section 4.1.3.1, and 2nd version refers the version described in section 4.1.3.2.
These tests are run on the 5 instantiations of the generic model, where the
strengthened invariants are not included and where the global model checker
is used (sal-smc). However for all the runs that succeeded without errors all
assertions were valid, meaning the safety properties hold for the specications.
==PAGE:114==
98
Experimental Results
We see that the completely naive approach performs worst, and that it cannot
handle the cross and typical examples. It is not surprising that this approach
performs worst, as we have redundancy in the expressions of the guards. How-
ever we also see that for global model checker the version described in section
4.1.3.1 performs better compared to the version described in section 4.1.3.2. Ap-
parently the introduction of extra transitions performs better compared to using
the DEFINITION keyword. However if one wants to do bounded model check-
ing using induction, the approach described in section 4.1.3.1, here described as
1st Version, cannot be used.
Also we see for the large examples that SAL runs out of memory for all the
dierent versions. For these large examples SAL runs for about 3-4 hours before
it runs out of memory and terminates with error.
9.2.4
Bounded Model Checking in SAL
For running the bounded model checker in SAL (sal-bmc) for the translated in-
stantiations of the generic model, one should use the version, including strength-
ened invariants, and where these invariants are concatenated into one assertion.
SAL has not been able to complete this for even the small examples using
bounded model checking. We get the error: Too many heap sections: Increase
MAXHINR or MAX_HEAP_SECTS. It seems that SAL runs out of memory
before it can nish checking the specications. For all the small examples, toy,
cross and typical, the tool runs for approximately 3-4 hours before giving the
memory error.
SAL has not been able to prove the safety properties for the example specica-
tions using bounded model checking.

checker, however for the toy, cross and typical example, this is possible using
the global model checker. In table 9.5 the running time of using SAL is com-

where the strengthened invariants are not included and where strengthened are
included. For SAL the global model checker (sal-smc) is used. Furthermore for

better, as in the RT-Tester tool, for instance bit operators are introduced to
represent the occupancy of sections and this should increase performance. Fur-

orities of transitions. This means the tool looks at a condition, and according
to this value of the condition, it only looks at a subset of the transitions in the
3 The test has been stopped after 52 hr without nishing. For this test SAL has been

==PAGE:116==

Experimental Results
system.
For the SAL specications, the tool still have to look at all the transitions, even
though we have introduced variables to describe which priority layers it can
choose transitions from. This introduction of these variables improve perfor-
mance in SAL, however it still needs to look at all transitions, as the priority of
transitions is implemented by appending an expression to the guards of lower
priority transitions. These expressions appended to the guards are quite simple
and these are appended in the beginning of the guard, but still SAL performs
signicantly worse compared to the RT-Tester tool.
Furthermore dierent approaches of model checking is used for SAL and the RT-
Tester tool. In sal, global model checking is used here, where bounded model
checking using induction is used by the RT-Tester tool for these results.
We also see for the SAL tool that the running time for the versions, where the
strengthened invariants are included are bigger compared to running time of
versions where just the safety properties are described. This is not surprising,
however we see that there is quite big dierence for the running times of these
two versions for the dierent examples.
In connection to this, it should be
noted that when running global model checker, it is sucient to include the
safety properties. The purpose of the strengthened invariants is to avoid an
invalid initial state for bounded model checkers using induction.
We also see that memory usage is bigger when the strengthened invariants are
included. In connection to this, it should be noted that we have an overhead
in memory by the occupancy is described by arrays. Due to the problem de-
scribed in section 8.3.1, the index type of the arrays PointPosVar, PointCmdVar,
DownToUp, UpToDown, StemToPM, PlusToStem and MinusToStem are set to
be SecId instead of just PointId or LinearSectionId.
==PAGE:117==
Chapter 10
Limitations
This chapter discusses limitations of the RSL-SAL tool. This chapter discusses
both limitations that is present in both the original tool and extended tool.
Furthermore it looks at limitations specic for the extensions of the tool.
10.1
Limitations of Existing Tool
This section describes limitation that applies to the original tool. These limita-
tions also applies to the extended RSL-SAL tool.
10.1.1
Domain and Range Types of Maps
The domain type and the range type of a map cannot be a complex type expres-
sion, if the specication is to be translated into SAL. For instance the following
type denition in a RSL specication
type
Mp = Int −m−> {| i
:
Int :−i
i s i n
{0 ,1 ,2}|}
==PAGE:118==
102
Limitations
cannot be translated into SAL. The RSL type checker does not complain about
this, but when translating into SAL, the user gets an error that the domain type
of the array is illegal. However the specication can be changed into:
type
MpRange = {| i :
Int :−{0 ,1 ,2}|} ,
Mp = Int −m−> MpRange
which will be translated successfully into SAL. So if one has a complex type as
domain or range type of a map, then one needs to specify this as a subtype and
then reference to the subtype as the domain/range type of the map.
10.1.2
Functions as Variables
The SAL translator does not support having variables of function type. For
instance, it is not supported to have the following
transition_system
l o c a l
f
:
Int −> Int := −\i
:
Int :−i
transition_system
f (1) = 1 ==> . . .
Functions can be dened for values but for variables, this arises errors. If one has
a specication like the above, here the type checker will succeed. The translator
will exit with ok code that nothing has gone wrong, but an internal error is
written on the screen for the user. The internal error tells the user that no
value denition with the identier f can be found. When running the sal-wfc
command to check wellformedness of the translated SAL specications, this will
fail for both non-cc and cc-versions, as this is not translated correctly.
Therefore one should not use function type for variables. This limitation also
applies to the extended tool.
10.1.3
Empty Types
SAL does not support denition of empty types.
For instance if one has a
specication including the following:
type
Subint = {| i
:
Int :−i
i s i n
{}|}
==PAGE:119==
10.2 Extended Tool
103
transition_system
[ System ]
l o c a l
a :
Int := 1
in
([=] i
:
Subint :−true ==> a ' = 2)
end
This can be type checked successfully and successfully translated into SAL, and
the wellformedness checker of the generated SAL code also terminates success-
fully. But when running the SAL model checker of the translated specication,
the tool terminates with a segmentation error. Empty types are not supported
in SAL.
This limitation also means that our generic model dened for Railway Systems
as described in chapter 8, only works for examples having at least one linear
section, at least one point, at least one route and at least one marker board, if
the example should be translated into SAL and checked with SAL.
10.2
Extended Tool
This section describes limitation of the extended tool.
10.2.1
Domain and Range Type of Arrays
For the domain and range type of arrays, we have the same limitation as we
have for maps as described in section 10.1.1.
Likewise if one wants to create a set type, where each element should be in the
subtype of integers in the specic set {0,1,2}, then one needs to dene a type
representing the subtype of integers in the set {0,1,2}, and use this dened type
to dene the set type. This limitation for sets also existed in the original tool,
but sets of subtypes was not handled correctly in the original tool (see section
6.4), meaning one should use sets of types that are not maximal types with care
in the original tool. For the extended tool, one should just be aware not to use
complex type denitions for dening a set type, like one should be for maps
described in section 10.1.1.
==PAGE:120==
104
Limitations
10.2.2
Update of Arrays in CC
For our cc-version, we use lifted types, which we also do for arrays. This means
that if we want to update a value for just a certain index i, we do this by applying
an array expression, where all indexes except for i is just given the value, they
initially had.
How we update the value of an array occurrence is described
in section 5.3.5.1.
This means that we can handle the dierent expressions,
however this way introduces some overhead, as we are not just assigning a new
value to a certain index, but we run through all indexes of the array. This we
need to do, since we use a lifted type in the cc-version.
10.2.3
Operations for Array Expressions
Only the assignment operation is supported for array expressions. If we have
two arrays, we cannot not directly check if the two arrays are equal. Let us say,
we have two variables a and b, which are both of array type, and both index
type Int and range type Int. And let us say we want to check equality of these
two variables, then it is not supported to write the following:
a = b
Instead one needs to write a quantied expression checking this property at each
index:
a l l
i
:
Int :−a [ i ] = b [ i ]
==PAGE:121==
Chapter 11
Future Work
This section describes how future work in connection to this project could be
performed.
11.1
Translation into Other Tool
For future work in connection to this, it could be interesting to write a trans-
lator into for instance the RT-Tester tool, as this tool is also used for checking
models according to the future danish interlocking systems. As the RSL-SAL
tool is extended to be able to represent generic model for the future danish
interlocking systems, a translator from RSL-SAL into internal code of the RT-
Tester tool could be interesting. This way the type checker of RSL-SAL could
be used for specifying a model for the interlocking systems. Especially since the
experimental results indicate that the RT-Tester tool performs better compared
to the SAL tool.
For writing a translator from RSL-SAL into another tool, one could use Gentle,
which is also used by the SAL translator. Then one would need to create an
abstract syntax three for describing a specication in the RT-Tester tool. After
this, one should write actions and rules in Gentle in order to convert an abstract
syntax three of a RSL specication into a RT-Tester specication.
==PAGE:122==
106
Future Work
11.1.1
Bit Operators
In connection to this, as bit operators are supported in the RT-Tester tool, the
RSL-SAL language could also be extended to accept bit operators. Using bit
operators increase performance, and since this is supported for the RT-Tester
tool, this would be an useful extension to the language for this. For adding the
support of bit operators to the language, rstly the ast.g le should be changed,
so that the abstract syntax of a RSL specication can contain bit operators.
These should be extended in the same way as other normal operators such as
plus or minus is already supported. However one should be aware that maybe the
bit operators should have a higher priority compared to other normal operators
between integers. The priority of the operators is described in grammar.g le,
and likewise this le should be extended, so that the concrete syntax accepts
bit operators, eg &, |.
11.1.2
Comparison with Other Model Checkers
Furthermore it could also be interesting to compare the performance of the RT-
Tester tool and SAL to other model checking tools. As we already have dened
generic model of future danish interlocking systems in RSL, one approach for
doing this, is to write a translator from RSL to such model checking tool.
Such translator could start by just support translating the subset of the RSL
tool that is used in our specication of the Railway System.
11.2
Extensions to Tool
This section describes possible general extensions to the RSL-SAL language and
SAL translator.
11.2.1
Functions as Variables
An useful extension to the existing RSL-SAL tool could be to allow variables to
have a function type. The RSL already supports having lambda expressions and
variables to be of function type. Likewise lambda expressions is supported in
SAL and in SAL a variable can be of function type. Likewise the SAL translator
already supports translation of lambda expressions.
==PAGE:123==
11.2 Extensions to Tool
107
For extending the tool into including this, one needs to change a bit in the
sal_gen_ast.g le.
Actually just by modifying the Gen_SAL_applic_Expr
action, one can include the use of function type for variables for the non-cc-
version.
This action now checks if the function is a value occurrence.
For
extending, this action should also check if the function is a variable occurrence.
For the cc-versions some further extensions needs to be done. Here one needs
to handle lifted type for function types. Like the translator create lifted types
for maps and sets (and also arrays for the extended tool), the translator must
also create a lifted type for the function type. This includes some extensions to
the sal_aux.g le, where we have actions for dening maps and sets. Likewise
this should then contain actions for handling function type. It might also be
necessary to create a prelude le for the function type, as we have for maps and
sets. This prelude le should dene general functions for handling the function
type.
However for the specic Railway System described in this report, we do not
need to support this, but in general this could be an useful construct for other
models.
11.2.2
If-then-else expressions
Like described in section 3.1.4, this could also be an interesting extension to the
language. By the specic Railway System, this is not used, but one might think
of other models, where this functionality is desired.
For extending the tool into accepting this, one can extend the TRANSITION_OPERATOR
type described in section 5.2.1.1 to include a functor for describing if-then-
else expression, which then should contain a VALUE_EXPR (value expression)
describing the condition, a TRANSITION_OPERATOR describing the then-
branch and a TRANSITION_OPERATOR describing the else-branch.
Also one should extend the grammar rules (grammar.g) for reading transition
operators according to the following:
t r a n s i t i o n s o p e r a t o r =
transitionsoperator_equal
|
transitionsoperator_greater
|
transitionsoperator_bracketed
|
guarded_cmd
|
t r a n s i t i o n s o p e r a t o r _ i f t h e n e l s e
==PAGE:124==
108
Future Work
t r a n s i t i o n s o p e r a t o r _ i f t h e n e l s e =
i f
value_expr then
t r a n s i t i o n s o p e r a t o r
e l s e
t r a n s i t i o n s o p e r a t o r
This way one can have a nesting of prioritized transitions and if-then-else ex-
pressions for the transitions.
11.3
Looking into Errors
When running the SAL specications, we ran into some errors. First of all, we
saw that SAL has trouble evaluating possible values of elds in record types,
which gives problems when we try to access arrays. As described in Section
8.3.1, we have problems in connection to this. This is dicult to do something
about, as it is a result of how SAL behaves. However one could further into,
how this could be eliminated.
Furthermore SAL runs out of memory when trying to check the generic model
for future danish interlocking systems for large examples. It could be interesting
looking into, if and how this could be eliminated. This requires looking into the
compiling of the SAL tool.
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 12
Conclusion
The RSL-SAL language has been extended to support arrays and prioritizing of
transitions. Likewise the translator from RSL-SAL into SAL has been extended
to include these extensions. Furthermore bugs of the existing RSL-SAL tool
and translator has been identied and xed.
A generic model for the future danish interlocking systems has been specied
in the extended RSL-SAL language. Five dierent example have been used to
instantiate the model, and the tool has been used to translate these instantia-
tions into SAL code. This way SAL has been used to check the model for the
ve instantiations.
The generic model can successfully be translated into SAL using the translator.
We see that SAL can be used to verify safety properties for especially the three
smaller examples toy, cross and typical. However for the bigger examples, gt-hd
and kj (kj corresponds to the danish train station Køge), we see that SAL runs
out of memory before, it can verify the properties.
Two dierent versions have been implemented for translating prioritized tran-
sitions into SAL as described in sections 4.1.3.1, where extra transitions are
introduced in order to update value of variables describing what priority layer
transitions should be chosen from, and 4.1.3.2, where the DEFINITION key-
word is used for ensuring correct value of the variables describing from which
==PAGE:126==
110
Conclusion
priority layer transitions should be chosen from.
According to experimental
results, we see that the global model checker performs best for the method of
introducing extra transitions, meaning the method of section 4.1.3.1. However
this approach is not appropriate when bounded model checking using induction
is used. When doing bounded model checking using induction, the method of
using the DEFINITION keyword as described in section 4.1.3.2 should be used.
From the experimental results comparing the performance of SAL and the RT-
Tester model checking tool, we see that the RT-Tester tool performs signicantly
better compared to SAL. This is probably not so surprising as for the specica-
tion of the model in the RT-Tester tool, for instance bit operators are used in
order to improve performance, and likewise if-then-else statements are used for
representing dierent priorities of the transitions, and this is not possible using
SAL. Likewise with the RT-Tester tool bounded model checking using induction
is used, and for the SAL specications it has not been possible to possible to
complete bounded model checking using induction, as SAL runs out of memory,
so we compare with results of running global model checker of SAL.
Furthermore we have seen that there has been some issues using the SAL model
checker tool. In particularly there has been some issues when accessing elds of
record types and access of arrays in the same transitions. This has lead to the
introduction of a bunch of auxiliary value denitions for the model not including
strengthened properties. For the model containing strengthened properties that
can be used for bounded model checking using induction, this has lead to specify
arrays to have a larger index type than should be necessary for the model.
Another issue is that SAL runs out of memory for large examples and for cc-
versions. For eliminating this, one needs to compile SAL with specications so
that the tool is allowed to use more memory. Further work could be to look into
this for trying to make SAL be able to deal with large examples. However we
see that for the large examples, in particularly the Gt-hd example, SAL runs
for long time, before it can nish.
==PAGE:127==
Appendix A
Cookbook For RSL-SAL
This is a cookbook for extending RSL. Firstly it is described, how one can extend
the syntax of RSL. Afterwards it is described what to do, if one wants to apply
the extension to the SAL translator.
A.1
RSL
If one wants to extend the syntax of RSL, then one should start by extend-
ing the abstract syntax of an RSL-specication, which is the le ast.g. One
should extend the abstract syntax to include types and functors to represent
the statements, expressions and/or types, one wants to add to the language.
Then one should extend the grammar rules for the concrete syntax, which is
done in grammar.g.
One should create actions and/or rules for reading the
new statements and/or expressions. These actions and rules should generate
elements of the types and using the functors, that one has extended to abstract
syntax three. After changes to ast.g and grammar.g the tool should be able to
read the desired expressions in the concrete syntax.
Furthermore the type checker should be extended, which is done in the le
values.g. The type checker should be extended to handle the new expressions
==PAGE:128==
112
Cookbook For RSL-SAL
and/or types. In connection to this, one might need to alter the types.g le as
well.
The print.g le should be extended in order for the tool to generate error mes-
sages. If the ast.g le is extended with new types and functors, then print.g
should be extended, so that it can turn these new types and functors into strings
according to error messages.
pp.g should be extended for pretty-printing to work for the extension. Again
pp.g should be extended, so that it can turn new types and functors of ast.g
into boxes (the pretty-printer turns the specication into a set of boxes which
then are printed).
A.2
SAL Translator
If one wants to extend the SAL-translator, one should rstly extend the RSL to
include the extension as described in the above section A.1.
Then one should extend the abstract syntax three of SAL in the le asl_ast.g
with types and functor, like one can extend the le ast.g.
Before a specication is translated from RSL to SAL, the specication is re-
solved, which is done by the cc.g le. Here one should extend the actions and
rules for resolving to be able to resolve the new expressions and types.
The sal_gen_ast.g le takes the abstract syntax three of a resolved specication
of RSL and generates an abstract syntax three of SAL. This le should be
extended to be able to translate the new expressions and/or types. In connection
to this, one might need to change other les with sal-prex as well. RSL-SAL
contains a bunch of les having the prex sal, where sal_gen_ast.g is mainly
responsible for translating into an abstract syntax three of SAL.
Lastly the sal_gen_code.g should be extended, in order for the tool to print
SAL-code representing the expressions. If one has dened a new type, sal_gen_code_types
should also be extended in order to print the new type expression as SAL code.
